        “The elders of Castle Ehtahl would like to ask you a few questions, Benjamin,” Bernard Zwirn said as he walked to a cabinet hanging on the wall.

        “Are these machines?”

        “Benjamin! There are our elders! Don’t insult them in front of them. Wait until you leave if you want to insult them.”

        “But, they aren’t human.”

        “No, they’re not. They are automatons.”

        “The elders of your castle are automatons?”

        “No. The elders of my castle are lazy. They’ve been replaced by automatons because they got bored of asking the same questions over and over.”

        Bernard Zwirn placed a quarter into a slot in the cabinet and then pulled a gigantic lever from the cabinet to the floor. As he did so, sparks burst from each of the automatons and they all made rickety noises as their tin and copper pieces shifted back and forth. They did not look graceful or smooth because their movements were quite jerky and their exteriors looked rusty in several spots. Some of the automatons did not have complete control over their movements causing their arms, lips, or legs to move in a peculiar ballet of metal and electricity.

        “What is your name?” one of the automatons asked in a metallic, crisp voice.

        Benjamin turned to face the automaton which had a pen in its crooked fingers.

        “Benjamin Buckingham,” Benjamin responded.

        “What is your identification number?” another automaton asked from a different direction.

        “Override! Outsider questions,” Bernard Zwirn said quickly. He gave Benjamin a nod of approval because Benjamin obviously did not have an identification number. The automatons shifted back and forth and jerked their limbs in various movements that together looked like they had been synchronized.

        “Where are you from?” another automaton asked.

        “New York.”

        “Where is Nu Work?” another automaton asked with mispronunciation.

        “In the United States?”

        “OK. We found New York,” another automaton answered. “Is your address 2788 Huckleberry Road?”

        “Yes,” Benjamin said with surprise. “How did you--.”

        “Are you nine years old?”

        “Yes,” Benjamin replied with even more surprise.

        “What is your relationship with Aloysius Trenhaile?” another automaton asked.

        “I don’t know who that is. Who is he?”
        “We don’t talk about it,” yet another automaton commented. “Who is the President of the United States?”

        “Barack Obama.”

        “And how did you get here?”

        “Bernard Zwirn took me here,” Benjamin responded.

        “Pause!” Bernard Zwirn said as he raised the lever from the ground to the cabinet, powering down all of the automatons in the process. “We want to know how you came to Ehtahl? How did you wind up in the castle in first place.”

        “Oh, that’s easy--”

        “Wait for the automatons to return,” Bernard Zwirn said as he powered the automatons to the jingle and jangle and clanking of metal parts.

        Benjamin relayed the story of how he got to where he was today. He mentioned running through Prague in his dream, waking up to find a dinosaur under his bed, following the dinosaur into an unknown land, seeing a gladiator battle, sitting in an office building, and everything that led to that moment. If machines could look as surprised as Bernard Zwirn and Noel Jaffin did, these automatons were as shocked by this outcome as anyone else. Benjamin took a deep breath after he finished.

        “Why are you really here?” an automaton asked.

        “I wanted to see where the dinosaur was going,” Benjamin said quietly.

        “Dinosaur?” Bernard Zwirn asked.

        “Yes. The tyrannosaurus rex from my dream.”

        Bernard Zwirn groaned through his teeth and rolled his eyes.

        “Oh, Rodney.”

        “Rodney! He kept saying that name.”

        “Yes, Rodney Cohen,” Bernard Zwirn said while shaking his head. “That guy gets us in so much trouble we’re not going to be able to do what we do much longer.”

        “What do you do?”

        “I’ll tell you all about it as we leave.”

        “Leave?”
        “Yes, Benjamin, we need to get you home.”

        “But, I don’t want to go.”

        “It’s not really your choice,” Bernard Zwirn said. “Elders, when does the next train leave?”

        The automatons moved back and forth in a more rigid, uncontrolled fashion. They jerked from side to side and steam started pouring from their heads, sending a screeching sound into the air. Joints started to disconnect and pieces of robots starts to fall all over the ground.

        One automaton leaned forward and moved its jaw slowly.

        “Ten minutes.”

        “Ten minutes!” Bernard Zwirn exclaimed. “Benjamin, we have to go!”

        “But where am I?”

        “I’ll give you a tour on the way. We have to run!”

Chapter 8: In Which There Is A Train

        Benjamin and Bernard Zwirn sprinted down one hallway, then another, and another until Benjamin lost a sense of which direction he was going. Everything felt disorienting and confusing. The more he saw of this castle, the less he understand it.

        “We’ve got nine minutes to get you to the train station,” Bernard Zwirn said in between breaths.

        “Why can’t I take the next train?” Benjamin asked.

        “The trains are too infrequent and I’ve got a busy schedule. If you don’t get on that train, I don’t know what we’re going to do with you here.”

        “I could keep to myself. I’ll just look around and see where all the dinosaurs live.”

        “Benjamin, you won’t be able to live here because this place is much different than where you’re from. This is, how should I say, a little bit like a manufacturing city.”

        “A manufacturing city?”

        “Yes, we make something here.”

        “Like bikes?”

        “Kind of,” Bernard Zwirn said as he opened a door to the outside world. “It’s a little bit more complicated than that.”

        “What do you make?”

        “Oh, my,” Bernard Zwirn said as he glanced at his watch. “We’re going to be late. We’ve got seven minutes.”

        Benjamin thought about stopping, so that Bernard Zwirn had no choice but to let him hang out in Castle Ehtahl. Or, Benjamin thought Bernard Zwirn would have to carry him to the train station. And since the king brought in Noel Jaffin to handle the task earlier, Benjamin was sure that Bernard Zwirn would not exert any physical strength to get him on the train. He was a large man but he looked like he had the strength of someone a quarter the size. The same, Benjamin felt, went for his organizational skills.

        “I’ll sprint to the train,” Benjamin said. “Because I’m a lot faster than you but in exchange, you have to tell me what you make here.”

        “Deal!” Bernard Zwirn said quickly, his brow covered in sweat and his chest heaving back and forth like rough waves in the ocean. “We make dreams.”

        “You make dreams?”

        “Yes, we make dreams.”

        “What do you mean?”

        Bernard Zwirn removed a necklace and held it to Benjamin. On the end, there was a small brass key that looked like it was much older than the king who wore it.

        “You have to use this key to get into the train station. You go straight towards that gigantic tower, then make a left and run along the river to a series of blue, yellow, and green buildings. The yellow building is the entrance to the train station and you’ll need this key to get inside.”

        “But, I don’t understand what you mean--” Benjamin began.

        “You have five minutes to run straight for that gigantic tower, then make a left and run along--” Bernard Zwirn began repeating the directions again.

        “No, sorry,” Benjamin replied. “What do you mean you make dreams?”

        “We make dreams.”

        “You make dreams come true?”

        “No, this isn’t New York, Benjamin. We make dreams. When you go to sleep at night and you have a dream, those dreams are manufactured here.”

        “Here?”
        “Yes, here. And if you stay here, your presence might throw off the balance of peace and we’re not going to be able to produce dreams for very much longer.”

        “I don’t understand.”

        “I don’t expect you to,” Bernard Zwirn said as he grabbed Benjamin by the shoulders. “You have to leave because if you don’t, this castle and the dreams it creates might be destroyed by nightmares. You have to run. The less you know, the better. If you want to have dreams of dinosaurs again, you need to sprint as fast as you can.”

        Benjamin wanted to dream of dinosaurs again and immediately turned from Bernard Zwirn and ran as fast as he could. Run for the gigantic tower, Benjamin thought to himself. It was impossible to miss the tower and Benjamin had already seen it from the office building. As he got closer and closer to it, he noticed it had far more moving parts than he had thought. Each individual part seemed to move independent of all of the other parts, as if it was the most complex watch ever created. He wanted to inspect the tower further but he also wanted to have more dreams so he ran through crowds to the bank of the river. As he turned left towards the yellow building, he slipped on the ground and the brass key fell from his grasp. Benjamin got to his knees and crawled five feet to it. He reached for the brass key and extended his fingers. But then, a heavy leather boot with rusty metal lining stepped on it. Benjamin tried to push the boot away but it would not budge. He looked up and he saw a face he had seen previously. It was Richard Fox.

        “Hello again, Benjamin,” Richard Fox said with a sly tone.

        “You’re standing on my key,” Benjamin exclaimed. “I have to get to the train station.”

        “Why do you have to go so soon?”

        “King Bernard Zwirn said I have to or else I’ll throw off the balance of dreams and nightmares.”

        Richard Fox shifted more of his weight onto the brass key.

        “He’s always saying that, you know?” Richard Fox said with a curious grin. He wore a heavy leather trench coat and a dark hat that seemed too hot for such a warm day. Benjamin tried to push his boot again but Richard Fox resisted further.

        “You have to let me go!” Benjamin yelled at him.
        “Don’t you want to stay?” Richard Fox asked with an uncomfortably villainous tone.

        “Yes, I do,” Benjamin said.

        “Then, stay!” Richard Fox yelled. “I give you my permission.”
        “But, King Bernard Zwirn said--”

        “Oh, King Bernard Zwirn is always saying these things. He wants the castle all to himself and he doesn’t want anyone to interfere. I bet he didn’t show you any of the castle, did he?”

        “Well, no, he didn’t. But, he seemed friendly enough and he told me that I needed to get home quickly.”

        “Of course he did. He wanted to get you out of the way so that he can make sure he can continue to manufacture dreams here. He doesn’t want you to learn how they’re made because that’ll mean he won’t be able to make them. I think you should stay, Benjamin,” Richard Fox said.

        “I want to leave!” Benjamin yelled. “Maybe you’re right but I don’t want to take any chances. I’m going home.”

        Benjamin pushed one more time on Richard Fox’s foot and the boot jerked away. The brass key looked delicate despite the heavy boot upon it. Benjamin grabbed the key and jumped to his feet.

        Yellow house, Benjamin thought to himself. He had to get to the yellow house.

        He ran past the blue building and then past the green building. At the yellow house, there was a keyhole in the middle of a large door that appeared to be the size of the brass key that Benjamin held. Benjamin jammed the key inside and was thrilled when he noticed it fit perfectly. He used the full weight of his tiny body to move the door open. Once there was enough room for him to squeeze inside, he found himself in the most beautiful, spacious, and grand train stations he had ever seen. There were people drinking coffee from balcony seats looking down on train conductors walking along the platform amid women selling flowers and men shining shoes. There was room for dozens of trains to pull into the station and the roof was high enough that it felt like it was an enclosed stadium. The outside of the yellow building did not foretell at all what Benjamin saw in the inside. He felt as if he had wandered into a time when trains were the most important technology of an era. And among these trains, Benjamin saw one train at the last gate.

        Benjamin felt it was inappropriate to be sprinting as quickly as he was through such a tranquil, beautiful setting but he also felt there was no other option to reaching the train in time. He was ten gates away when he heard the conductor start the steam engine, nine gates away when he heard the wheels begin to churn, eight gates away when the cars started to tug at each other, seven gates away when he heard the train whistle, six gates away when the wheels made their first rotation, five gates away when the locomotive began leaving the station, four gates away when the first passenger car left the station, three gates away when half the train had left the station, two gates away when the locomotive whistled again, and one gate away when he watched it leave the station altogether.

        He missed the train.

        He did not know what it meant to miss the train. There wasn’t another train in the station but, presumably, he could just get on the next one and be home in time.

        “No!” Benjamin heard a man scream from the entrance of the train station. “What are you doing here?” yelled Bernard Zwirn. “You were supposed to be on that train!”

        “I tried,” Benjamin said between large breaths. “I ran as fast as I could but it left.”

        “You should have run faster,” Bernard Zwirn said with an increasing amount of anxiety. “This isn’t good, Benjamin. This isn’t good at all.”

        “I’ll get on the next train,” Benjamin said. “If there’s one this afternoon, I’ll take that one. If there’s one this evening, I’ll take it. Or even if I have to wait until tomorrow, I’ll take that one.”

        “Benjamin, the next train is in a month!”

        Benjamin’s face turned white as he imagined what his mother was going to say to him.

        “A month?” Benjamin asked quietly.

        “Yes, a month! You have to wait a month!”

        “I can’t wait a month!”

        “I know! You can’t wait a month! That’s why you need to be on that train,” Bernard Zwirn yelled to no one in particular.

        “Why aren’t there more trains?”

        “People don’t leave Castle Ehtahl very often,” Bernard Zwirn said as he sat on a bench next to a dozen dogs barking at a bird sitting on a standing clock. “We don’t get visitors.”

        “I tried really hard to get to the train station on time,” Benjamin started to say, becoming a little scared and sad about the situation that he now found himself in. “I didn’t try to miss the train.”

        “It happens,” Bernard Zwirn said. “It’s fine. There’s nothing we can do about it now.”

        “I was going to make it but then Russell Fox stepped on the brass ring and I couldn’t get it quick enough and then he delayed me by at least two minutes.”

        Bernard Zwirn stood from the bench and looked even more concerned than he had been previously. The dogs next to him ran away and the bird on the clock took flight. The entire station went quiet and still.

        “What happened?” Bernard Zwirn asked very slowly.

        “Russell Fox stepped on the brass key,” Benjamin responded very quietly.

        “How do you know Russell Fox?”

        “I saw him in the hallway when I was waiting to talk to Pritchett.”

        “And he prevented you from getting to the train station in time?”

        “He stood on the key.”

        “Did he say anything else to you?”

        Benjamin thought hard about this question and was worried that he was taking too long to respond. Everything Richard Fox had told him had been about Bernard Zwirn, and Benjamin was not sure if any of it was true. He did not know where he was and did not want to put himself in a situation where he felt in danger. He needed to quickly assess who could be his friends and protectors until he could get on the next train.

        “He said you wanted me to leave,” Benjamin said.

        “That’s correct. I do want you to leave, for your own protection,” Bernard Zwirn responded. “Did he say anything else?”

        The answer to these complicated questions did not come quick enough for Benjamin. He knew that Bernard Zwirn knew that Benjamin was stalling.

        “He said you did not want anyone else to make dreams,” Benjamin said each word while trying to observe Bernard Zwirn’s response.

        “Russell Fox!” Bernard Zwirn yelled into the air. “Benjamin, for the next month, you are not to talk to that man. You have to understand me. This is for your own protection. Russell Fox is not a good person these days. He was good, at one time, but those days are gone. This is a difficult time for Castle Ehtahl and I don’t want you to get wrapped in the middle of it. I need you to obey my orders and I’ll get you safely out of this castle in a month.”

        Benjamin swallowed hard and with his instincts felt that he had to trust Bernard Zwirn more than he trusted Russell Fox. Bernard Zwirn wanted Benjamin to leave and there was no harm in leaving. But, Russell Fox wanted Benjamin to stay and Benjamin did not know if there was harm in staying.

        “Pritchett told me you came here because you didn’t want to go to school,” Bernard Zwirn said as they strolled for the exit of the train station. “Well, you’re going to have to attend school here.”

Chapter 9: In Which There Is A School

        Benjamin tossed and turned in his bed, hoping that a gigantic wave would send him flailing into a gigantic mast, that dinosaurs were on an adventure with him to stop Professor Undutois, and that he could be among his friends. But as he continued to roll about, he could not dream. He thought, perhaps, it was because he was in a different environment. This was not his bedroom, after all. It was a large room filled with ten other students who all lived in the same dormitory and attended the same class. Benjamin sat on the edge of his bed and remarked how everyone else was able to sleep peacefully. They must be having amazing dreams of far off places and incredible experiences and gladiator battles and being heros. All he got from his first night sleeping in this foreign place was exhaustion.

        The lights flickered on and the door at the end of the room opened, revealing a tall, skinny woman who looked much more refreshed than Benjamin felt. Benjamin presumed that this was his teacher, Estelle Lindenmeyer, because she looked very similar to how Bernard Zwirn had described her. She had light red hair and blue eyes slightly hidden by yellow glasses. Her hair lay on her shoulders and she always wore light color clothing like whites and off-whites and off-off-whites. And as she spoke, Benjamin understood why Bernard Zwirn referred to her as the nicest person in Castle Ehtahl.

        “OK, time to wake up,” Estelle Lindenmeyer said to all of the boys in the room. She then left, closing the door halfway, and walked to the other end of the hallway. Benjamin could hear the soft pattering of her shoes hitting the wooden floor as she took each step.

        “OK, time to wake up,” he heard Estelle Lindenmeyer say faintly, most likely to the room that the girls occupied.

        Benjamin was already awake because he barely slept. It was uncomfortable being in a new place with new people and he did not like that he did not have the privacy of his own bedroom. When he thought about the adventures that he was about to embark upon, he did not imagine that in some cases, the circumstances would be less inviting than his circumstances at home. He had his own bedroom at home, after all.

        The boys stood, yawned, rubbed their eyes, and did the various other body movements that these creatures do in the first minutes of the beginning of a new day. Everyone looked similar to his class at home. People must not be that different, Benjamin thought to himself.

        “Move,” the boy on the bed next to Benjamin said.

        “Hi, I’m Benjamin,” Benjamin replied, trying to make his first friend.

        “You’re on my shirt. Move,” the boy said as Benjamin realized that he was standing on a shirt that the boy wanted to wear.

        “Oh, sorry,” Benjamin said as he moved his foot. He had obviously made it dirty and was not thrilled about having unsuccessfully made a new friend.

        “Who are you?” the boy asked as he rubbed his eyes.

        “My name’s Benjamin. What’s your name?”

        “Ralph,” the boy answered as he stood and walked to the exit. “But, I’ve never seen you before.”

        “I’m new. I was supposed to leave on a train yesterday but I missed it.”

        “So, you’re in our class now?” Ralph asked with a confused face.

        “Yes, I guess so.”

        “Weird. Where are you from?”

        “Boys! Let’s go,” Estelle Lindenmeyer said as she entered the door again. Benjamin was glad that she interrupted their brief, not very cordial conversation because he was not sure how to answer the question without causing alarm.

        Each of the boys walked passed Estelle Lindenmeyer and as they did so, the teacher checked off their name with a pencil on a paper attached to a clipboard. The boys walked through and Estelle Lindenmeyer acknowledged their presence. But then, she saw that Benjamin was still in the room and that he was having a little trouble tying his shoes.

        “It’s not so bad, once you do it a thousand times,” Estelle Lindenmeyer said as she walked towards him. “You must be Benjamin.”

        “Hi, I’m Benjamin,” Benjamin said awkwardly. He was still trying his best to make a good first impression with everyone.

        “King Bernard Zwirn told me all about you. My name is Miss Lindenmeyer and I’ll be your teacher, for the next month I presume,” she said so softly and warmly that Benjamin immediately knew that if no one else, he could trust her the most.

        “I didn’t mean to miss the train,” Benjamin offered as he did not want to be seen as irresponsible.

        “Oh, it’s fine,” she said as she escorted Benjamin through the exit. “Our train schedule is goofy anyways. So, King Bernard Zwirn tells me that you’re in the fifth grade. Is that correct?”

        “Yes, I’m in fifth grade. My teacher is Mr. Marchetta but he isn’t very good.”

        “Oh no?” Miss Lindenmeyer said with more curiosity than Benjamin expected. “What don’t you like about the way he teaches?”

        “He doesn’t really pay attention to what the kids do in the classroom. I don’t think he can see, to be honest. He looks like he’s staring far off into the distance.”

        “Well, every teacher has a different teaching style,” Miss Lindenmeyer said as she positioned Benjamin at the end of a line of about twenty students his age. “I think you’re going to find that my teaching style is a little different than other teachers you may have. For one, we spend a lot of time teaching and thinking about dreams because this is where dreams are made. You’ll be fine and if you have any questions or if you get nervous about anything, let me know and I’ll help you.”

        Benjamin felt more relieved that his teacher was so much nicer than Mr. Marchetta. Miss Lindenmeyer already knew Benjamin’s name and made him feel so calm that he began to think he might actually enjoy going to school here.

        The students walked forward in a single line and Benjamin saw that they were all dressed in the same uniform. The boys had similar haircuts and the girls wore their hair in a similar style. The line itself alternated boy and girl, which Benjamin found amusing because his school was not as organized as this school.

        As he walked forward, the girl in front of him turned and gave Benjamin a confused face. He expected to receive these types of expressions on the first day. These children already knew each other and Benjamin would be the new person. It was fine, Benjamin thought, because he was used to feeling like the odd person left out.

        They continued walking forward and the girl spun around again, this time giving Benjamin a confused expression that bordered on anger. Benjamin did not know how to respond so he smiled cautiously. It was not his best smile. It may have been his worst, though. He was not sure but he was trying to smile somewhere in between his best and his worst smile. Never before in his life had he concentrated on such things before. He was determined to be liked and to be average.

        The girl spun around once more and stared at Benjamin, then shook her head from side to side. Benjamin smiled again, this time showing more teeth than he wanted.

        “Hi,” Benjamin said quietly so as not to cause the entire line of students to turn around.

        “Who are you?” the girl asked precisely. Her diction was perfect but her manners were abrupt. Benjamin worried that he was interacting with the bully in the classroom.

        “My name’s Benjamin. I’m new.”

        “I know you’re new. If you weren’t new, I wouldn’t have asked you who you were.”

        “I suppose that’s right.”

        “We don’t say things like that so you must not be from here. Where are you from?”

        “I’m from New York.”

        As soon as the word New York left his mouth, all of the students stopped walking. There was a collective, loud gasp of surprise followed by books and backpacks falling to the ground. Several students hid behind other students while the larger of the bunch tried to move the line further and further away from Benjamin. He could feel their nervous, tense reaction as he watched them stare at him with wide eyes and lowered jaws.

        “OK, children,” Miss Lindenmeyer said as she walked to the back of the line. “I guess now is as good a time as any to introduce our new student. This is Benjamin Buckingham from New York. He’s going to be with us for the next month as a foreign exchange student. Everyone, please be nice and give a warm hello to Benjamin.”

        “Hello, Benjamin,” the class said in unison and in a worried, confused voice.

        “He’s from New York,” Ralph whispered to Miss Lindenmeyer loud enough that Benjamin could hear this.

        “Yes, right, so Benjamin isn’t from Castle Ehtahl. Benjamin’s from New York,” Miss Lindenmeyer said.

        The class reacted exactly as they had when they heard Benjamin say the word. There was gasps, books dropping, children cowering, and lots of fear.

        “And, that’s OK. We’re going to treat him like he’s from Castle Ehtahl. He’ll get along fine here.”

        “But, we can’t. He can’t know what we learn here,” the girl in front of Benjamin said with total concern.

        “Selma, it’ll be OK. He’ll learn enough to make dreaming more interesting but he won’t learn too much that he won’t enjoy dreaming any longer. A month will be too short a time for anything crazy to happen. Now, let’s keep going to the classroom,” Miss Lindenmeyer said as she gently guided each student back into a single line.

Benjamin felt some nervous looks and glances from the other students. He did not think it was going to be easy to become friends with everyone now. No one seemed to like New York, he thought, and he didn’t quite understand why.

When Benjamin entered the classroom, he knew that he had entered a school in which he had never been before. There was color everywhere. Paper trees lined the back wall with branches that spread to the ceiling to reveal a blue sky with clouds hanging over desks. Flowers and animals lined the other sides, drawn to a detail that Benjamin thought looked so realistic. Each chair was a different color and each desk had multiple color combinations. It was like he had walked upon a painter’s color pallet.

Benjamin sat in the back of the classroom, behind Selma Applebaum who had only moments earlier revealed that Benjamin was not from here. He did not like sitting near the class bully but he was content that at least, he was sitting behind her. As Benjamin thought about this some more, he was a little disappointed that he had already discovered which student was the bully. He was hoping that he could enter a classroom where there was no bully.

“Today, we’re going to continue our lesson on creating dreams for grumpy old men!” Miss Lindenmeyer told the class as she drew two old men on the chalkboard. There were some giggles throughout the classroom. “Per our lesson last week, who wants to tell me the three important rules that you have to keep in mind when you create a dream for a grumpy old man?”

Every student stretched their hand to the sky but Benjamin noticed that Selma was stretching her hand much higher than all of the other students. She was almost standing as she stretched her body further and further. He had never been in a classroom where there had been such eagerness to answer a question.

“Steven, why don’t you give me the rules,” Miss Lindenmeyer said to a larger boy who was sitting next to Ralph Eden, the boy whose bed was next to Benjamin’s.

Steven stood from his chair and counted the rules on his finger.

“First, the dream can’t be too crazy because the grumpy old man could have a heart attack and that’s not good,” he said.

“That’s right,” Miss Lindenmeyer said as she wrote crazy dream equals heart attack on the board.

“Second, the dream can’t be too happy because the grumpy old man will become even more grumpy.”

“Right,” Miss Lindenmeyer said as she wrote too happy equals more grumpy.

“And third, the dream can’t happen in too many different places or the grumpy old man will get lost.”

“The first two were correct,” Miss Lindenmeyer said. “But, I think you’re confusing the third one with one of the rules for creating a dream for women who get turned around easily. Do you know the third rule for grumpy old man dreams?”

Eric stood and shrugged his shoulders. And as soon as his shoulders started to rotate around, Selma’s hand shot back into the air again. She wanted to answer the question more than any other student in the room and Benjamin realized that Miss Lindenmeyer was deliberately not calling on her. But since no one else had a hand in the error, there was no one else to answer the question but Selma.

“OK, Selma,” Miss Lindenmeyer said in a way that sounded like she said these words several times every day. “What’s the answer?”

“Don’t include anything in the dream that made the grumpy old man grumpy in the first place,” Selma said as fast as she raised her hand.

“That’s right,” Miss Lindenmeyer said as she wrote grumpy memories equals grumpier old man.

Benjamin was impressed that Selma knew the answer but he did not understand what it meant. Who were these grumpy men, he wondered, and why were these kids talking about the rules for dreams. In his classroom at home, they only talked about their own dreams. It was confusing but he did not want to ask basic questions in front of everyone because he knew he was the only one who didn’t know the answer.

“Benjamin, are you able to follow along?” Miss Lindenmeyer asked as if she was reading his mind.

“Kind of,” Benjamin said quietly. “But, how do you make dreams?”

The class laughed and Benjamin immediately regretted asking the question.

“That’s a perfectly fine question, Benjamin. The class is laughing because they never thought to ask such a question. They’ve been watching dreams be made their entire lives. Basically, we make dreams inside studios throughout the castle. Each person who has a dream, like the grumpy old man, has different rules assigned to them. So, that’s why it’s important that we learn them here.”

Miss Lindenmeyer turned to the chalkboard and began to clear the chalkboard. Benjamin wasn’t completely sure what she meant because it felt so foreign to him. People didn’t make dreams. Dreams were something that happened in his head while he was asleep.

“Did you understand that?” Selma asked Benjamin in a way that suggested she wanted to give Benjamin more explanation.

“I think so,” Benjamin said with a confused tone. “But, not really.”

“Good,” Selma said. “Because I study the hardest in this classroom and I work harder than everyone else and I’m not about to let someone from New York come in and ruin it all for me.”

Benjamin was taken aback by her attitude and did not quite know how to respond. He did not want to agitate her further.

“I’m not here to ruin anything. I missed my train.”

“I bet you did,” Selma said as she spun around and raised her hand to answer Miss Lindenmeyer’s next question.

As Selma walked to the front of the classroom to draw a layout for a dream in which a grandmother parachutes over a length of the Amazon jungle, Benjamin thought about switching seats to be closer to Ralph and Steven. There was an empty seat near them and Benjamin had decided that it was in his best interest to be friends with them rather than Selma.

“And then you have to make sure a fire extinguisher is available for each of the actors because there’s a chance that the grandmother could catch on fire,” Selma concluded.

“Well done,” Miss Lindenmeyer said as the rest of the class groaned slightly.

“Selma knows all about fires because her dad broke the dream machine,” Steven said as he gave Ralph a high-five.

Selma stared at Steven and Ralph as she slowly made her way back to her seat. Benjamin found it peculiar that in this classroom, some students would make such a comment to the class bully. He could never imagine a situation when he or anyone else would make a comment like that to Howard Horne.

“Not nice, Steven,” Miss Lindenmeyer said. “And remember, your mother has made her fair share of fires as well.”

The rest of the class laughs in a way that Benjamin doesn’t quite understand. He was also a little distracted because as bright as the room was, it was starting to get darker and darker. Over the course of a few minutes, the room became so dark that Miss Lindenmeyer had to turn on a light switch near the door. But, it was still early in the afternoon. The sun was still out, Benjamin observed through the window.

His curiosity overwhelmed him and he raised his hand to find an answer.

“Yes, Benjamin,” Miss Lindenmeyer asked.

“Why did it suddenly get dark?” Benjamin asked as he looked outside.

Before Miss Lindenmeyer could answer, there was a loud scream from outside the school. It was loud enough to cause Benjamin to worry that someone was in trouble. But inside the classroom, no one said anything and no one looked out the window. Everyone continued to stay seated as they had before.

“We don’t talk about it,” Miss Lindenmeyer said with her lips clenched together.

Chapter 10: In Which There Is A Bully

Benjamin lay awake in bed staring into the darkness above him, wondering how worried his parents were that he was not at home. He also wondered if Mr. Marchetta even knew that he was gone. Being away from his family was causing him to miss them more; but being away from his school had quite the opposite effect. In fact, if it was not for his family, he could see himself living somewhere else indefinitely. Castle Ehtahl, on the one hand, may not have been the best choice as his first destination. For if nothing else, he was not able to sleep well there at all.

“Pssst,” a voice in the bunk next to him sounded. “You awake?”

The voice was none other than Ralph Eden, who Benjamin had become accustomed to knowing as the other boy in the dormitory who did not sleep at night. Ralph frequently used his flashlight to read books that were prohibited on school grounds. The only books the students were allowed to read during their personal time were books that had been written with incredible creativity and imagination. Books that downplayed excitement and fun were prohibited. They were the equivalent of poisoning the brain. They belonged to the nightmares.

“Yes,” Benjamin said without moving. “I’m usually awake.”

“Why don’t you sleep?” Ralph asked.

“I don’t know how to sleep here,” Benjamin replied. As the words left his mouth, he realized this was true. When he was home, he could easily prepare himself to sleep by imagining his favorite dreams. But here, he was forgetting them and was unable to prepare himself to sleep. He couldn’t save the world from Professor Undutois.

“It takes awhile to get used to not being able to dream,” Ralph said.

“Not being able to dream?” Benjamin rolled to his side and stared into further darkness. “You can’t dream?”

“I could months ago but I haven’t had a dream since then. My dreams were amazing. I was conquering land after land and building bigger and bigger castles.”

“What happened? What did you stop dreaming?” Benjamin asked.

“I didn’t choose to stop dreaming. When our dream machine broke and we learned we only had a limited supply of dreams left, everyone in the castle had to give up their dreams.”

“You gave up dreaming?”

“Yep. And, that’s why you can’t dream either,” Ralph said. “There’s no more dreaming here. No more dreams until the machine is fixed.”

“So, why don’t you just fix the machine?”

Ralph laughed and Steven Wazen on the other side of the dormitory shushed to tell him to be quiet. Steven could sleep as well as all the other students in the room.

“We can’t just fix the machine,” Ralph said. “If we could fix it, don’t you think we would have fixed it?”

“Yes, I guess. I mean, I would hope that would be the case.”

“Right. We can’t fix it because it’s broken and there’s a piece missing.”

Benjamin knew the appropriate question to ask next but decided to let silence handle the conversation because he did not want to be seen as dumb.

“So, your next question,” Ralph continued. “Must be why don’t we just make another piece.”

“Yes,” Benjamin answered.

“And the answer to that question is that the dream machine has over one hundred thousand pieces. We do not know which piece is missing, at least that’s what the parents in town tell us.”

“You don’t believe them?”

“It’s not that I don’t believe them. But, if this machine has been here for centuries, surely someone must have written down all of the pieces.”

“Maybe there is a blueprint for how to make another one,” Benjamin suggested without really knowing what he was saying.

“There is,” Ralph replied quickly. “But, Aloysius has it.”

“Aloysius!” Benjamin said loudly. Steven Wazen on the other end of the room shushed more loudly than he had earlier. Benjamin lowered his voice and in tandem, tried to lower his enthusiasm for learning more. “Who is Aloysius?”

“We don’t talk about it,” Ralph said between his teeth.

“Everyone keeps saying that,” Benjamin said. “But, I don’t understand why.”

“If you knew why, you would understand why we don’t talk about it.”

Benjamin rolled onto his back again and continued to stare into the darkness. He thought about Aloysius Trenhaile over and over and over. Without any details to create him in his mind, he tried to imagine what a person would look like that everyone else would not want to talk about. The only person that came to mind was Howard Horne. And, Benjamin decided, not thinking about anything was much better than thinking about Howard Horne.

Footsteps slowly made their way closer and closer to Benjamin’s bed until a figure stood at the end.

“You guys have to be quiet,” Steven Wazen said with disgust. “Just because you can’t sleep, doesn’t mean we can’t.”

“Sorry,” Benjamin said quickly. “We’ll be quiet.”

“Were you dreaming?” Ralph asked with a smirk.

“I was dreaming of dreaming,” Steven replied with a laugh.

As Steven returned to his bunk, Benjamin closed his eyes and decided that although he was happy not being at school, he wanted to be able to dream. And that in addition to missing his family made him realize that he needed to go to Bernard Zwirn in the morning and find out how he could take an earlier train home. This place was fine but it felt like there was not much adventure and that he would be happier if he went back.

The lights in the dormitory flickered on and off until they stayed on. Benjamin looked at the large clock on the wall and noticed that it was four o’clock in the morning. It was two and a half hours earlier than they normally woke up. The lights never turned on before it was time to start the day.

All of the boys sat on their beds and looked at the clock with even more worry than Benjamin had. They looked at each other and every one of them had an identical curl in the brow, each mirroring each others confusion.

“Have to wake up!” a brash voice yelled at the end of the dormitory. “Have to wake up now!”

Benjamin looked and saw Noel Jaffin, Bernard Zwirn’s body guard, standing in the doorway with heavy armor covering his body. He looked like a metal cube, heavy enough that he could not be moved easily. And although the sight of a metal cube person was rather funny, Benjamin was a little unnerved that everyone else was unnerved. Noel Jaffin did not wake up the boys. That was Miss Lindenmeyer’s responsibility. And when Miss Lindenmeyer woke up the boys, she did not appear dressed in armor. Noel Jaffin was not prepared for a day of learning. He was prepared for battle.

“Wake up! Wake up! Wake up!” he said as he pounded his short legs back and forth into the ground.

Slowly, boy by boy arose and got changed.

“Hurry!” Noel Jaffin yelled to the young children. “This is not a drill!”

“A drill?” Benjamin said aloud.

“We were told a day like this might happen,” Ralph said with a tiny feeling of exhaustion.

“What might happen?” Benjamin said. “What’s going on?”

“Just follow orders,” Ralph replied. “And, you probably will be fine.”

Benjamin was confused and now he was certain that he wanted to go home. Even if this was the adventure that he was waiting for, he decided that he no longer wanted it. He wanted the idea and dream of adventure rather than the actual reality of it. Waking up at four o’clock in the morning was not an adventure. Seeing a guard who had previously tried to tackle him dressed in battle gear did not inspire adventure that Benjamin wanted. He was fine with bland adventures, he decided.

The boys lined up and Noel Jaffin checked their names on a sheet of paper on a clipboard as they passed him. In each case, Noel Jaffin had to stretch his neck backwards in order to see which child was which. As usual, Benjamin was last.

“I’ve decided that I’d like to go home,” Benjamin said as if Noel could somehow make this a reality.

“You’re not going home,” Noel Jaffin said quickly and matter of factly.

“But, I’d like to speak with King Bernard Zwirn about other ways that I could get home other than taking a train,” Benjamin continued.

“You’re not taking a train home,” Noel Jaffin said angrily.

“No, I am going to take a train home,” Benjamin said. “But, I’d like to find a way to go home earlier.”

“You’re not taking a train home and you’re not going home anytime time soon. King Bernard Zwirn has disappeared and without him, you’re going nowhere.”

Benjamin stopped. All of the boys in the line stopped. It was worse news that any of them had thought they would hear.

“What do you mean, disappeared?” Steven asked forcefully.

“I mean that King Bernard Zwirn had disappeared. I am here to take you to your class. No more questions. Silence while we walk,” Noel Jaffin said as he marched to the front of the line.

They marched, albeit at a slow pace due to Noel’s short stride, from hallway to hallway in complete silence. But despite not being able to hear them, Benjamin could feel that something had gone so wrong that these otherwise happy and energetic children had suddenly become scared and concerned. And that, unfortunately, made Benjamin even more scared and concerned because he knew less than anyone else.

As they made their way past the classroom, Noel Jaffin led them to another hallway decorated with paintings on all walls, ceilings, and even the floor. Benjamin stepped carefully from painting to painting of old men and women who lived in this castle previously. And oddly, each expression on their face looked more dire than the expressions he had noticed on their faces before.

Noel reached a painting of a woman wrapped in a red flag on a white horse whose two front legs flew into the air against a background of tan rocks that matched tan clouds. She pointed to the top left hand corner of the canvas. And that was where Noel Jaffin punched his fist through the canvas and pulled on a hidden lever, revealing an old circular staircase leading down.

Each of the boys filed one after the other while Noel Jaffin, again, checked their names against a piece of paper on a clipboard. Benjamin, again, was the last to depart but he decided this time that it was not in his best interest to ask Noel any other questions. Instead, he wondered how many other secret passageways existed in this castle. And, he wondered how many other secrets were there in general.

As he stepped down each curved stair, he imagined that they were descending several floors below the ground. It was dark, lit only by candles on the walls, mounted against cold rocks that were moist from whatever rain water had fallen below. After having turned six or seven times, Benjamin found himself at the end of turning. He stood at the opening of a massive, cavernous room that looked like it could hold as many people as he ever thought existed. And, it did. There were people everywhere.

In front of him, however, was Miss Lindenmeyer with the girls from the class. As Miss Lindenmeyer welcomed the boys to join the girls, Selma Applebaum took a step forward. Benjamin knew, before she said anything, that she was the angriest of everyone in the room.

“This is all Benjamin’s fault!” she screamed.

Chapter 11: In Which There Is A Flying Machine

        Benjamin felt his body freeze. His face had no color and his arms felt like cement. He had just been blamed for King Bernard Zwirn’s disappearance. But worse than having been blamed, Benjamin did not understand how he could have been responsible. All that he knew at that moment was that he was not liked and that feeling that once again he had no friends.

        “If Benjamin wasn’t here,” Selma Applebaum screamed as loudly as she could. “This would never have happened!”

        Miss Lindenmeyer restrained Selma and pushed her to the back of the room. Benjamin did not know where to look. He wanted to find a friend, a dinosaur, amid the room but realized that he was all alone.

        “Can I go home?” Benjamin muttered quietly to Miss Lindenmeyer.

        “It’s not your fault, Benjamin,” Miss Lindenmeyer said as she cautiously pushed Benjamin towards the rest of the class. “You didn’t understand what coming here meant. You didn’t do anything wrong.”

        “Did King Bernard Zwirn disappear because of me?”

        “Of course not, Benjamin. I would say that he would have disappeared whether you were here or not,” Miss Lindenmeyer said calmly.

        Benjamin looked around at the other students but none of them wanted to make eye contact. It was as if all of the kids around him thought that Benjamin would disappear if they did not see him.

        “I’d like to go home,” Benjamin whispered to Miss Lindenmeyer.

        “I wish you could,” Miss Lindenmeyer said. “But, you’re going to be stuck here for a little bit.”

        Although they were in a different location in different circumstances under different moods, Miss Lindenmeyer held her class as she had before. Benjamin sat behind everyone else who circled the teacher in that dark cavernous space. It was difficult for anyone to pay attention because there were people walking around and talking about what may happen next. The distractions were endless but for Benjamin, holding class was a welcome return to normalcy.

        “Today, we’re going to play with flight machines. We normally wouldn’t but this space just so happens to have one of the tallest ceilings around, which makes it incredibly safe. No one is going to be flying to the moon,” Miss Lindenmeyer said.

        The students were happy about the idea of using flight machines. Benjamin did not know what they were but he was intrigued when Noel Jaffin wheeled a long clothes rack of odd shaped machines to Miss Lindenmeyer. They looked like a combination of a motorcycle engine and a broomstick, each of them painted green.

        “Thanks, Noel,” Miss Lindenmeyer said. “Now students, grab a flight machine. Make sure you get your correct size.”

        Benjamin watched all of the students pick their flight machines and when it was his turn to pick his, there was only one flight machine left. He was not sure if it was his size or not. So, he took it and hide behind the other students.

        “Oh, Benjamin,” Miss Lindenmeyer began to say with some caution.

        “Benjamin shouldn’t be allowed to use the flight machines,” Selma said sternly to Miss Lindenmeyer, without addressing Benjamin himself.

        He took a step toward the clothes rack to return the flight machine.

        “It’s fine,” Miss Lindenmeyer said quickly and abruptly. “Benjamin, watch Selma put on her flight machine.”

        Selma was quite upset that she had suddenly been partnered with Benjamin. And Benjamin was quite concerned that his safety was being entrusted to someone who hated him. It was not a great partnership by any means.

        Benjamin watched Selma quickly settle into her flight machine and turn the ignition switch on. It made a louder noise than Benjamin expected but was not so loud that he needed to cover his ears. It hummed like a mechanical hummingbird would.

        “Selma, we’re going to fly one at a time,” Miss Lindenmeyer said. “Because we don’t want any more mid-air collisions, right Ryan?”

        Miss Lindenmeyer had the students line up in front of her. One by one, she inspected the flying machines to make sure that they were working properly. She did not want a disaster to happen. A student could fly uncontrollably through the large room or even worse, a student could fly uncontrollably and hit everyone else in the room. Benjamin watched her inspect the machines like a mechanic had inspected his parents’ cars at home. Then when Miss Lindenmeyer was finally satisfied that everything appeared to be in order, she went to the first student and nodded with approval.

        Steven Wazen flew into the air as his flying machine’s engined churned a fine, soft buzz. It was not like the sound of a motorcycle or the sound of a locomotive. It was like the sound of a microwave and a kettle of boiling water. Benjamin watched Steven turn the flying machine to the left and then to the right and then to the left again. He flew fast, then slow, and then fast again. It looked almost graceful, Benjamin thought, as student after student flew into the air and around the room.

To Benjamin, the entire ordeal, so far, was both exhilarating and frightening. This was actually some of the adventure that he was hoping to find. He wanted experiences that were different than his experiences at home and certainly, there were not many flying machines at home. And yet when each student successfully landed following an adrenaline-filled flight, he became more and more scared as he realized it would next be his turn.

        “OK, Benjamin,” Miss Lindenmeyer said with the same confidence and enthusiasm she had said to all of the other students. “Let me show you how this works.”

        Miss Lindenmeyer sat on the flying machine, pressed an ignition button, and then slowly ascended into the air.

        “Once you get this high,” Miss Lindenmeyer shouted as she began to turn around in the air. “You lean forward to go forward. You lean backward to go backward. Left to go left and right to go right. It’s actually quite easy.”

        “Is it magic?” Benjamin asked.

        “No!” Selma shouted from behind him. “This isn’t magic. This is science.”

        “Selma’s right,” Miss Lindenmeyer said as she landed smoothly and then promptly adjusted her hair. “It’s just science. We have a lot of machines here that you probably don’t have in New York. They help us create more vivid dreams.”

        Benjamin wanted to stall a little longer. He was not feeling as confident as the other students felt about flying one of these machines. He wanted a machine that could make him brave. Even if he was able to get the flying machine into the air, he felt he would then be defenseless and visible to all of the people on the ground who currently hated him. Certainly, someone would throw a rock or some fruit, he thought.

        “I don’t think this is a good idea, Miss Lindenmeyer,” Selma protested as she flew into the air and coasted along the walls of the room.

        “It’ll be fine, Benjamin,” Miss Lindenmeyer reassured him. “The machine does most of the work for you. Now, just have a seat.”

        She nudged Benjamin into position, tied his seatbelt tight, and then fastened his helmet. As soon as he looked like someone who would soon take flight, Miss Lindenmeyer hit the ignition button without warning Benjamin that she was going to do so. He felt a sudden jerk of the machine and a sudden beat of his heart. The machine raised Benjamin further and further into the air where Benjamin first had a glance of the number of people in this space. It was tens of thousands over a length larger than his neighborhood. Although he still felt more comfortable on the ground, flying was more fun than he expected. It was also scary.

        As Miss Lindenmeyer advised, he moved forward as he leaned forward. He moved backwards as he leaned backwards. The machine sensed his movements and handled everything for him.

        He even grew enough confidence that he relaxed his tight grip on the handlebars and looked to the students below. Selma Applebaum, along with the rest of the class, had their arms crossed in front of them and their lips crossed as well. Benjamin wondered how long the battery would last because he would not mind flying home.

        “OK, Benjamin,” Miss Lindenmeyer yelled. “I’m going to turn off the machine from my remote control. You’ll descend in a second.”

        Miss Lindenmeyer took out her phone and clicked a few times until the engine of the flying machine stopped. Benjamin waited to get back to the ground, entrusting his teacher to his safety. His teacher, though, looked at Benjamin with confusion and then looked back at her phone with more confusion.

        “It’s off,” Miss Lindenmeyer said. “You should be ascending.”

        Benjamin looked to the rest of the class whose contorted faces had suddenly taken on the same confused look that Miss Lindenmeyer showed. He was, he knew, descending to the ground. He was ascending and go further and further into the air.

        Miss Lindenmeyer became more worried and she ran for a closet on the near side of the room. Benjamin looked to see where she was going and the flying machine turned with him. He pointed the machine down and it started to go down. Maybe, he thought, she just did not have cell phone reception in this dark hall.

        He watched his teacher return with a long rope. She then turn one end of it into a lasso. The other students had uncrossed their arms and now had their hands on their hips. Benjamin did not know what to make of their expressions other than something peculiar was happening.

        “Catch the rope when I toss it to you,” Miss Lindenmeyer yelled.

She threw the lasso up in the air and Benjamin grabbed it when it was near. She pulled and pulled and pulled with all her strength and Benjamin descended to the ground. As soon as he placed his right foot on the ground, the flying machine jerked and fell suddenly to the floor.

        Miss Lindenmeyer was exhausted, Benjamin saw. And the students, they were perplexed.

        “OK, everyone,” Miss Lindenmeyer said as she continued along with her class. “Let’s get lunch.”

        Everyone filed in a single line and started to march onward. As Benjamin passed Miss Lindenmeyer, she placed a hand on his chest so that he he had to stop walking forward. She looked from side to side as if she did not want anyone else to hear what she was about to say. Benjamin leaned forward so that he could hear her whispers.

        “You can’t leave my side or view,” Miss Lindenmeyer whispered. “You were able to fly without the machine. You’re in a lot of danger.”

Chapter 12: In Which There Is Evil

        Everyone in class treated Benjamin a little differently. He noticed that Ryan Eden and Steven Wazen watched him as if they were hawks staring at a little mouse. Selma Applebaum refused to make eye contact with Benjamin but he knew she was affected by him. It was impossible to ignore a student who was able to fly a machine when it was not powered on.

        “That hasn’t been done before,” said one student. “How did you do it?”

        “I don’t know,” Benjamin said. “I just kept sitting.”

        “Can you do that with other machines?” another student asked.

        “I don’t know,” Benjamin responded again. “I don’t know what other machines exist.”

        “Are you related to Bernard Zwirn Senior?” another student asked.

        “No, I don’t think so,” Benjamin answered.

        “That would be amazing if you were,” another student stated. “Imagine we have in our class a student who is a relative of the man who made all of the dream machines.”

        “Bernard Zwirn made all of these machines?” Benjamin asked.

        “His dad did,” Selma said as she continued to look forward at the chalkboard while all of the other students continued to look backward to Benjamin. “But, he’s gone.”

        “And Bernard Zwirn is his son?” Benjamin asked.

        “Yes,” Selma said. “He’s one of Bernard Zwirn’s three sons.”

        “Who are the other two?”

        “We don’t talk about it,” everyone said in exhausted unison.

        “What’s life like in New York?” another student asked.

        “It’s fun,” Benjamin said. “It’s not too different than living here I suppose.”

        “Are there people everywhere?”

        “Yes.”

        “And lots of food?”

        “Yes.”

        “And people can do whatever they want.”

        “For the most part.”

        “And everyone has dreams?”

        “I think so,” Benjamin said. “But, I guess you would know better than I would.”

        “Students,” Miss Lindenmeyer said as she entered the classroom wearing a raincoat. “We have to go to the general assembly area for an announcement. So I need you to line up in a single file and head outside. Raincoats on!”

        Student after student put on their coats and filed from the classroom outside into the unforgiving rain. There were people exiting what seemed like every building, all headed in the same direction toward the gigantic dream machine. Benjamin wondered what all of these people did each day. He did not know what every person in New York did and imagined students felt the same way about Castle Ehtahl. There were tens of thousands of people of various ages and sizes and colors gathered in one big plaza. The dream machine hovered over them like a tree that had spiralled upward without casting a single branch.

        Benjamin followed Steven Wazen and noticed that Miss Lindenmeyer, who led the group of students, frequently turned around to make sure that she could see him. When they reached a spot in the plaza with enough space for the class, Miss Lindenmeyer positioned everyone in a couple rows and then stood next to Benjamin.

        “You are not to leave my side,” Miss Lindenmeyer said.

        “What is happening?” Benjamin asked.

        “I’m not exactly sure,” Miss Lindenmeyer responded. “But, it’s probably related to Bernard Zwirn’s disappearance.”

        “Does this happen often?”

        “No,” Selma replied. “Bernard Zwirn liked talking to small groups of people rather than everyone at once.”

        “So, who is going to speak now?” Benjamin asked.

        Selma shrugged her shoulders as if she did not care. At the front of the plaza, before the tens of thousands of people, was a green and blue stone building that towered almost nearly as high as the dream machine. The building reminded Benjamin of cathedrals he had seen pictures before. But, this building was much wider and much more detailed than any cathedral he had ever seen. At the very top, there was a balcony with a red flag hanging over the entrance.

        As the rain continued, projection screens were hastily opened throughout the plaza. Cameras turned on and focused themselves on the balcony. Lights flickered and flashed from all directions as sound checks began to test the limits of the speaker system. A hush came over the crowd as Castle Ehtahl’s anthem began to play. It was a song that reminded Benjamin of jazz songs his dad would play on the radio while working on the family car. The rhythm was quick and the song was incredibly upbeat, Benjamin thought. It felt inspiring, even though Benjamin knew none of the words.

        When the song ended, the lights were dimmed and a roar spread across the crowd from the back to the front. Even Benjamin got caught up in the excitement of what was to come. He noticed, however, that Miss Lindenmeyer was not energized at all. Her eyes were glancing from left to right in a fast enough manner that suggested that she was looking for someone. Benjamin realized she was nervous and that made him nervous. He trusted Miss Lindenmeyer more than he trusted the crowd.

        On the balcony, the red flag was removed by two guards who looked to be much more qualified for their position than Noel Jaffin. The crowd erupted in anticipation like Benjamin had seen at music concerts on television. Benjamin expected, for reasons he could not explain, to see Bernard Zwirn enter.

        A man did enter but it was not Bernard Zwirn. Benjamin did not know who it was but he did know the man standing immediately behind him. That was Russell Fox.

The crowd erupted in excitement and cheers and Benjamin was more confused. He did not see any reason to cheer for someone who was friends with Russell Fox. No one else must realize that Russell Fox was the reason that Benjamin was still there.        

        “My fellow Ehtahlians,” Russell Fox projected into a microphone. “I present to you the interim King, Thalia Nachamie.”

        The crowd screamed and cheered. Miss Lindenmeyer did not flinch and Benjamin noticed that Selma Applebaum did not flinch as well. The other students in the class cheered as loud or louder than the people around them. And at that moment, Benjamin began to realize that everyone may have been cheering only because everyone was cheering and not because of the men who stood on the balcony.

        Thalia Nachamie was tall and skinny with a long face and a crooked nose. His dark black hair was seamless with the black clothes he wore and the black hat on his head. He looked, if Benjamin could name an animal, like a large crow that wore pieces of leather rather than feathers.

“Ladies and gentleman,” Thalia Nachamie’s voice boomed across the plaza as the rain continued to pour down. “A great tragedy has come to our castle. Our very own Bernard Zwirn has been kidnapped. We do not know where he was taken and we do not know who has taken him. But, we will find him. And until we do, we will continue to do what we do best.”

Cheers erupted throughout the crowd and Benjamin’s face turned pale as he heard that Bernard Zwirn was kidnapped. It was, he thought, one of the worst possible answers to where the king may have been. Kidnapping, to Benjamin, meant that there was someone as evil as evil could be in this castle. Bernard Zwirn held the key to Benjamin’s departure and Benjamin began to feel queasy.

“We are going to set a search party to look for Bernard Zwirn. We don’t have many clues so if anyone knows what has happened, please contact me directly and I’ll make sure to tell you whether your clue is valid or not. As you all know, if Bernard Zwirn is not found in a week, we will have to move on without him. We cannot wait around endlessly for his return because our dream machine is running low.”

Thunder cracked in the distance and the rain became more fierce. Miss Lindenmeyer placed a hand over Benjamin as her eyes continued to move back and forth. But then, they stopped. Benjamin saw that she was looking to the left but he could not see what she had found.

“If anyone has any questions at this time, please ask them now,” Thalia Nachamie projected to the audience.

“Is there a chance anyone else could be kidnapped?” Russell Fox asked into a microphone behind Thalia Nachamie.

“That’s a great question,” Thalia Nachamie continued. “And there is a severe chance that every one of you could be kidnapped. So, it is important that you continue to do your jobs well and that you do not wander outside of this castle. If we cannot fix the dream machine, we will have to use another proposed solution. This proposed solution, as I told you last year, will make sure that you can make dreams and not be kidnapped.”

        “Will the dream machine be fixed?” Russell Fox asked into the microphone again. Benjamin could see that Russell Fox was reading from a tiny phone.

        “That is again a great question,” Thalia Nachamie began. “We are missing a piece to the dream machine and we do not know what this piece is or where this piece is. It’s possible that the dream machine has suffered damage beyond repair. But, we do not have any way of knowing whether we can fix it without having this piece returned to us. If you know the evil, villainous person who has stolen it, you must tell us immediately. We will not stand for evil in our castle.”

        “What will we do if we run out of resources to make dreams?” Russell Fox asked again, in the same tone and awkward reading style as the previous two questions.

        “Another great question,” Thalia Nachamie started. “We will not run out of resources to make dreams. If the dream machine fails, I will make sure we have the resources to make more dreams. You can count on that. I think that’s it for questions. Have a great day everyone.”

        Thunder crashed and Benjamin was surprised that Thalia Nachamie ended his question and answering session because there were over a hundred hands raised throughout the plaza. Selma had her hand raised too and took it back down with disgust. Miss Lindenmeyer was still looking to her left and Benjamin noticed that she was trying to talk to someone by mouthing words and reading lips. He still could not see who she was speaking with.

        As the crowd started to depart in various directions, Miss Lindenmeyer ushered the students back to the school where she was met by another teacher Benjamin had not seen before. Benjamin started to walk into the school but then Miss Lindenmeyer grabbed his hand. He saw that Selma was watching everything that was about to transpire.

        “Quickly, Benjamin,” Miss Lindenmeyer said. “Come with me.”

        Miss Lindenmeyer and Benjamin ran back into the crowd, which made Benjamin quite nervous. He did not know who to believe and who to trust other than Miss Lindenmeyer. Any of these faces looking upon him could belong to the person who kidnapped Bernard Zwirn. They zigged and zagged between one dark clothed body and another, getting wet from the rain along the way.

        Benjamin stopped. In the distance, he saw him.

        It was Snark.

        “Miss Lindenmeyer!” Benjamin yelled. “That’s the dinosaur that led me here!”

        Miss Lindenmeyer looked in the direction that Benjamin pointed. He was thrilled to know that his reason for adventure was not lost. He could, in fact, figure out what the dinosaurs did here. They were among him.

        But, unexpectedly, Miss Lindenmeyer was not as excited by Benjamin’s discovery.

        “Rodney,” Miss Lindenmeyer said with a long sigh. “We have to run, Benjamin.”

        She grabbed his hand as a person would who was pulling an unwilling partner. Benjamin looked back over his right shoulder and watched Snark walk the other direction. The dinosaurs were here, he thought. He could find his friends and they could take him back home. They knew where he lived, he said to himself.

        After having passed thousands of people, Miss Lindenmeyer approached the woman who she had been exchanging words with earlier. Benjamin knew her as well. It was Pritchett Pollock.

        “You need to take him,” Miss Lindenmeyer said to Pritchett Pollock. “He isn’t safe with me.”

        “We can’t talk here,” Pritchett Pollock said as she glanced from side to side at the thousands of people walking among them. “Let’s go to my office.”

        Pritchett Pollock and Miss Lindenmeyer started to walk in the same direction with Benjamin following. But as quickly as they began to walk, Pritchett put her hand out and leaned in to Miss Lindenmeyer.

        “Walk a different direction than me,” Pritchett Pollock whispered. “Meet in my office in twenty minutes.”

        Benjamin was pulled by Miss Lindenmeyer back into the crowd. He looked for Snark but didn’t see him. He was here, though, and that was all that mattered.

        When they eventually came to Pritchett Pollock’s office, Benjamin remarked that it was as organized as the day when he first saw it. He also noticed that Pritchett Pollock was staring at the pool of water Benjamin had brought into her office from the weather outside. He pushed it aside with his foot to no success. Pritchett Pollock took a towel from the window shelf and wiped the floor dry.

        “Pritchett, you have to hide Benjamin,” Miss Lindenmeyer said. “You’re the only one here that can hide someone.”

        “Hide him? For how long?” Pritchett Pollock answered.

        “You have to hide him until we can find Bernard Zwirn and his key. Benjamin has to be on the next train home.”

        “Why? What happened since I last saw him? Kids wind up here all the time, especially kids who had the luck of meeting Rodney,” Pritchett Pollock said with a little sarcasm.

        “Benjamin can fly on a flying machine without power. And, he’s from New York.”

        Pritchett Pollock’s stern face turned the slightest bit aware and more open. She tilted her head up and to the side to indicate that she was listening and wanted to hear more. These subtle adult movements drove Benjamin nuts. He did not understand why they could not just ask for more information.

        “I was teaching the class to fly on the flying machines,” Miss Lindenmeyer continued. “Benjamin went up as well and I controlled his flying machine from a remote control. He didn’t know that at the time. When I turned it off and had Benjamin come back to the ground, he continued to fly around.”

        “Maybe the remote control was busted,” Pritchett Pollock snapped back.

        “It wasn’t. I tested it again that evening.”

        “Maybe there were strong winds.”

        “We were indoors.”

        “Maybe it was a fluke.”

        “Pritchett, it wasn’t. And if Thalia Nachamie learns of this, Benjamin is going to be in incredible danger. Thalia Nachamie will either enslave him or kill him.”

        Pritchett Pollock touched her chin with her hand and examined Benjamin and the pool of water he was continuing to create on the floor. He was not sure why Miss Lindenmeyer trusted Pritchett Pollock so much with this information. But because he trusted Miss Lindenmeyer, he was starting to trust Pritchett Pollock a little more.

        “Fine,” Pritchett Pollock said. “I’ll hide him.”

        “Good,” Miss Lindenmeyer said. “Do you know where Bernard Zwirn is?”

        “Aloysius Trenhaile kidnapped him. It’s too dangerous to try to get him back,” Pritchett Pollock replied. “We may not see him again.”

Chapter 13: In Which There Is A Production Studio

        Since Benjamin had already been to Pritchett Pollock’s office, he did not find anything remarkable about it at all. In fact, it was more boring than how boring he found it last time. And this time, he had to sit quietly while Pritchett Pollock typed on her computer. It was dreadful.

        Some of the books on the shelf to his right looked interesting but as soon as he reached his hand over to take one, Pritchett Pollock’s fingers stopped tapping on the keyboard and her eyes stared at him until he returned his hand to his lap. He was so bored that he even thought about untying and tying his shoes over and over. But as soon as he leaned over to untie them, the keyboard typing stopped again. If he took a deep breath, the typing stopped until he exhaled again. If he stretched his arms, the typing stopped until he stopped stretching. And if he blew air through his lips like he was making the sound of a bumble bee, the typing stopped. It was the most boring place Benjamin had ever been.

        “Obviously,” Pritchett Pollock began. “This isn’t working.”

        “I’m bored!” Benjamin said as he jumped to his feet.

        “Sit,” Pritchett Pollock said. “And, I can see that you are bored.”

        “I saw one of the dinosaurs from my dreams in the plaza and I want to go find him. Will you help me find him?”

        “I have to work, Benjamin,” Pritchett Pollock said.

        “Can I go find him and then come back here?”

        “No, you’ll get yourself in a lot of trouble.”

        “What can I do?” Benjamin said as he sunk into the chair again. “I’m so bored and this is boring and this is not the adventure that I wanted.”

        Pritchett Pollock looked at Benjamin in a way that suggested that she knew that she was not going to get any work done while he was in her office. Benjamin looked at Pritchett Pollock in a way that suggested she was not going to get any work done while he was in her office. They both knew that they needed to do something else.

        “OK, fine,” Pritchett Pollock said as she daintily wiped any possible dust from her blue jacket. “I have to go to the control tower in an hour anyways so how about I give you a tour.”

“A tour!” Benjamin jumped to his feet in excitement. “I’ve been wanting a tour all along.”

Pritchett Pollock took a deep breath and sighed heavily, just as Benjamin had done when he was sitting in the chair while she typed. He was far more excited about the idea of a tour than she was.

“Are we going to go see dinosaurs in the wild? Maybe space shuttle launches? Will we see massive trolls yelling at one another?” Benjamin said as he swung his arms from side to side.

“We are going to see production studios.”

Benjamin stopped his motion and tilted his head to the side.

“Production studios?”

“Yes, Benjamin,” Pritchett Pollock said as she pulled an umbrella from behind her desk as well as an orange vest. “I’m going to show you where we make dreams. Before we leave, put this vest on so that I can see you at all times.”

The vest looked similar to what constructions workers wore when they worked on the television cable lines in Benjamin’s neighborhood. There did not seem to be anything special about it. But, Benjamin was beginning to understand there was a lot more mystery around here. He inspected the vest from the inside and out. Pritchett Pollock rolled her eyes.

“It’s just a vest,” she said.

Benjamin and Pritchett Pollock left her office building and entered another building immediately next door. Like Miss Lindenmeyer, Pritchett Pollock’s eyes moved back and forth quickly whenever they were around other people. Pritchett Pollock walked a lot faster than Miss Lindenmeyer and Benjamin almost had to jog to keep up.

“This is production building two thousand seven hundred and eighty eight,” Pritchett Pollock said as they walked down a brightly lit white hallway with hundreds of white doors on both the left and right. The hallway extended so far that Benjamin could not see the door on the other side. It was also so bright that he had to cup his hands around his eyes to see.

“Two thousand seven hundred and eighty eight?” Benjamin asked. “How many production buildings are there?”

Pritchett Pollock stopped and looked to the ceiling as if she was counting them in the air.

“I’m not sure,” she said quickly as she returned to her fast walking pace. “They come and go but somewhere around six billion I think.”

“Six billion!”

“I think so. I actually forget. I guess I should know. But, I don’t. The actual number isn’t that important.”

“What is behind each of these doors?” Benjamin asked as he reached for a handle.

Pritchett Pollock’s hand grabbed Benjamin’s wrist tightly.

“Don’t!” she said sharply but then upon realizing her tone, calmed herself quickly. “Don’t open the doors, Benjamin. I’ll show you what is inside.”

They continued walking down the hallway and by Benjamin’s count, Pritchett Pollock stopped in front of the twenty-ninth door on the right hand side. She placed her hand on the doorknob and then looked Benjamin in the eyes in a more gentle way than Benjamin had seen from her so far. It was calming to Benjamin whose heart was racing with anticipation of what was behind these mysterious door.

“This is where a dream is created,” Pritchett Pollock said as she opened the door and pushed Benjamin inside. She held her fingers to her lips and gestured to Benjamin to remain silent. He immediately understood why he had to wear the orange vest because there were many other people wearing orange vests as well.

But Benjamin was focused on everything else. There was a large speedboat cruising as fast as it could go through a river that raged through a metropolis. A person sat in the driver’s seat with a large smile, the wind throwing his hair back, and then sun bouncing light off his face. There were cameras everywhere capturing every angle and cranes holding lights and speakers above the stage. The speed boat roared as it switched gears and rocked back and forth as waves crashed into it. It went underneath bridges and passed sailboats and turned suddenly and headed the other direction. The metropolis looked like a metal forest that the speed boat moved through as quickly as it could. People in orange vests were moving the set as the man in the speed boat stood and then screamed with joy. Benjamin felt the joy the man must have been experiencing as everything here looked like so much fun. He wanted to ride a speed boat through a city.

        Pritchett Pollock opened the door to the hallway again and pushed Benjamin outside.

        “That was amazing!” Benjamin shouted into the hallway. He only realized then how much his voice echoed in this space. “Can I do that?”

        “I’m glad you enjoyed that, Benjamin. But, that’s someone’s dream. You can’t just do that.”

        “What do you mean?”

        “Someone is dreaming right now and that is his dream being played out. That was a production studio. Everything inside was orchestrated to make sure that person had a great dream.”

        “But, I never saw cameras or lights or speakers or people wearing orange vests in my dreams. Do my dreams come from here?”

        “Yes, they do,” Pritchett Pollock said as she walked faster down the hallway. “In fact, your dreams are the most expensive and technically involved that we have to produce. You don’t see the cameras or lights or speakers or people wearing orange vests because no one dreams in orange. You don’t see anything in orange in your dreams.”
