        “Well,” Myrna said as she folded the map and returned it to Benjamin. “That is certainly the worst drawn map I’ve ever seen. First of all, those don’t even look like trees. And secondly, you’re lucky to have found this place to begin with. How did you know which way was north?”

        “A squirrel ran by screaming North! North!”

        “Oh, Gerald!” Myrna said between her teeth. “He’s so noisy.”

        “How is it that you and a squirrel can talk?”

        “How is it that you can talk?”

        “Someone taught me to speak, I suppose. My parents taught me.”

        “Well, some people taught us to speak, too.”

        “That’s amazing! Who?”

        “So long as you don’t say where you’re from again, I’ll explain a little bit. Were you in a castle?”

        “Yes, Castle Ehtahl!”

        “Right, OK. So, there are these castles that make dreams. Castles for day dreams and castles for nighttime dreams. But, there are also castles that make nightmares and really, really bad dreams. Castle walls that make dreams keep the nightmares outside and keep the dreams inside. You are in between castles. This is where things or people go when they aren’t wanted.”

        “Not wanted?”

        “I’m going to presume that you did something that’s led you to be kicked out of Castle Ehtahl. Otherwise, they would have sent you back to,” Myrna looked side to side and then whispered. “New York.”

        “Well, I didn’t do anything but I was accused of doing something.”

        “OK, same thing. You’re out here like the rest of us. All of us have either done something or been accused of doing something and now no one wants us. So, we live out here.”

        “What did you do, Myrna?”

        Myrna stared Benjamin in the eyes, standing approximately three inches tall.

        “I killed a guy,” Myrna said sternly. “No, I’m kidding! I have to have fun out here. I don’t get to see that many people. No, no, I ate an apple and apparently it was someone’s very special apple and they were all like ‘you ate our apple!’ and I was like ‘it was delicious’ and now, here I am. It’s not a bad life. I’ve got all these informational brochures ready to give to people who journey through.”

        Benjamin could see that Myrna did not want to be where she was. She was not as happy handing out informational brochures as she said. He knew she wanted to be back in whatever castle that had kicked her out.

        “So the space in between castles, that’s the space for things in transition. We’re not wanted by the people who knew us. And, we haven’t found anyone else who wants us yet.”

        “Maybe you could apologize?” Benjamin offered.

        “Apologize for eating the apple? Oh, Benjamin, if only it was that easy. I’m fine here. It’s not so bad. I do miss my family but that’s another thing and let’s get you on your way.”

        “Your fam--”

        “So, the easiest way out of here is straight through that forest. After that, you’re going to have to get back into a castle so that they can get you out of here. Since you’re from,” Myrna leaned over and whispered, “New York, that means you’re going to have to find the person who welcome you here in the first place.”

        “That’s Bernard Zwirn.”

        “OK, so you have to find Bernard Zwirn and then he’ll send you home.”

        Myrna Delacourte stacked several maps and brochures together and handed them to Benjamin.

        “Would you like a coupon book for the shopping outlets on the west side of the forest? Actually, I’m just going to include it and that way you have it. Better have it and not need it, right?”

        “So, I need to go straight through the forest.”

        “Yes, go straight through the forest and then hang a left. You’ll see signs and follow the maps. One more thing about the forest, though. You need to be aware that the trees grow and move so you need to grow and move with them. Don’t get caught on your heels.”

        “Bye, Myrna,” Benjamin said as he walked past the booth. “I hope you get to see your family again.”

        “Bye, Benjamin. If you come across any apples, make sure to let me know.”

Chapter 20: In Which There Is A Tree

        Benjamin had been walking for the better part of the afternoon before he realized that he could not yet see an exit to the forest. It seemed to him like he might be walking in circles. Or worse, it seemed like the forest actually went on forever.

        He was surrounded by trees hundreds of times taller than he, with large branches that blocked his view of the sky. Brown leaves rustled on the ground as green leaves combed the air above. If he did not need to find the exit before the sun set, he would have enjoyed the peace and calm of everything around him.

The trees were extraordinary. Benjamin stood small and insignificant compared to their size. They climbed thousands of feet into the air like wooden skyscrapers.

        All of this was fine except that he wanted to leave, soon. The forest was the first of many things on his map that he received from Myrna. After the forest, there was a waterfall. Then, there was a river. Then, there was a prairie. Then, there was a mountain range. Then, a volcano. And finally, there was another castle that had information on how to leave.

        There were some berries on the ground and Benjamin decided that he needed to learn whether he was walking in circles or not. He gathered the berries and started to smash them against the trunk of a tree. As he tried to do so, the tree seemed to shake back and forth in such a way that none of the berries stuck. He grabbed some more berries and tried to smash them into the tree again. And again, the tree shook from side to side and the berry juice flew back into Benjamin’s face. Thinking that perhaps that tree had some sort of infection, he gathered more berries and tried to smash them into the trunk of another tree. And, the same thing happened. The tree shook the berries off it. He didn’t understand what was happening but decided he would try one last time.

        He gathered as many berries as his hands could hold and ran to the tree with the widest trunk in all of the forest. He held the red fruit above his head and as hard and fast as he could, he swung his arms into the tree. The berries smashed into the trunk and then flew back into Benjamin’s face just as fast as he had thrown them into the tree.

        “What is your problem!”

        Benjamin looked around. He didn’t know who asked that question but it sounded like a woman who was not too far away from him. It was a voice much deeper than Myrna’s and it echoed throughout the forest.

        “You can’t take a hint!” the voice yelled again. “Do you think it’s fun to have some little creature run up to you and smash berries into your side. Don’t you know berries stain?!”

        “I know berries stain,” Benjamin said as he moved his head back and forth to try to find the source of the voice. “That’s why I used them.”

        “You were throwing berries into my side on purpose!”

        “Who are you? Where are you?”

        “You’ve got some nerve. I thought you’d get the gist if I shook them away but for some reason, you had to run back with more berries. More berries! Why would you think I would want berries in my side?”

        “I’m trying to mark the trees so that I know whether I am on the right trail or not.”

        “And you think throwing berries into my side without asking my permission is the answer to your problem?”

        “I think it would solve my problem, yes. If I saw the berries again, I’d know that I was walking around in circles.”

        “But, the only problem is that I wouldn’t keep the berries on my side. They stain! Why would I want them on my side?!”

        “He tried to put them on me too,” an older deep manly voice echoed from another side of the forest.

        “He did what?!” the woman’s voice yelled.

        “He smashed berries into my side as well,” the man’s voice said.

        “Well, this is just a berry massacre, isn’t it?” the woman said. “This little boy has been trying to smash berries into the side of all of us.”

        “Me too,” a young girl’s voice said.

        “The children!” the female’s voice said. “You’ve even tried to smash berries into the children!”

        “I shook at least a dozen times and there are still some berry on me,” the elderly man said. “It’s going to take at least a few washings until I’m able to get this stain out.”

        “This is just an awful day,” said the woman. “There’s berry stains everywhere. Don’t you know how difficult it is to get berry stains off!”

        “I have no idea,” Benjamin said honestly.

        “Ask your mother,” the woman said. “They’re impossible. Downright impossible.”

        “Look at me,” said the young girl. “I’m pretty much an embarrassment at this point. I’ve got berry in my trunk that’ll take weeks to get out.”

        At this point, Benjamin realized that he was talking to the trees but he was still moving his head back and forth because he couldn’t understand where the voices were coming from. And so, he wasn’t sure if the trees themselves were talking to him or if there was a group of people who were creating an elaborate joke. He didn’t think it was an impossibility.

        “Who are you all?”

        “Oh, now he wants introductions,” the elderly man’s voice said.

        “Little boy comes into the forest and makes a mess of everything and now he wants to know our names,” the female voice said.

        “Kids these days are so rude. They’ve got no manners and think they can throw berries wherever they want.”

        “You’re preaching to the choir, Earl.”

        “So, his name is Earl,” Benjamin said quickly, referring to the elderly voice.

        “Oh shoot, I’m sorry Earl,” said the females voice while the young girl giggled in the background.

        “Ah, you’ve done it again,” said Earl. “You were never good at keeping secrets, Bertha.”

        “Bertha?” Benjamin asked.

        “So now you’re going to make fun of my name?” Bertha asked sarcastically.

        “Well, no, I was just confirming that your name is Bertha.”

        “Yes, it’s Bertha. That’s Earl. I’m Bertha. And, that’s Gertrude. You know all our names so now you know who to send money to for our cleaning bill.”

        “I’m Benjamin. I’m sorry I stained your trunks.”

        “Benjamin, eh?” said Earl. “I would have thought you looked a bit more like a robin.”

        “Robin, yes,” said Bertha. “I’ve seen many robins before and you do look a lot like them.”

        “I’m trying to find my way from the forest. I’ve been walking around for hours.”

        “We’ve been watching you,” Gertrude said. “And you’ve been walking around longer than hours. It’s been a couple days.”

        “Days?!” Benjamin said in surprise. “But, the sun hasn’t set yet.”

        “It doesn’t set over this forest. It just keeps circling and circling and circling.”

        “That’s exactly how I felt I was walking around. In circles.”

        “You were.”

        “See! I would have learned this had I put berries on the trunks of trees.”

        “You could have learned just as well if you just asked!” said Earl.

        “Well,” Benjamin said. “I didn’t think I’d get an answer if I asked a question into a forest.”

        “That’s true,” Bertha said. “There’s that group in the midwest of the forest that’s rather rude. They probably wouldn’t have told you. You should have put berries on them.”

        “Where am I now?”

        “You’re in the north east. Can’t you tell by our sophistication?”

        Benjamin couldn’t tell. They looked like any other trees.

        “Alright, fine,” Bertha said. “But, you’re going to have to believe us. We’re very, very sophisticated.”

        “Can you help me leave the forest?” Benjamin asked the towering natural wonders around him.

        “That depends on where you’d like to go,” Earl said.

        “I’ve got this map and it says I’ve got to find a waterfall on the side of the forest opposite the side I entered.”

        “I see,” said Bertha. “Did you get that map from Myrna?”

        “Yes, I did.”

        “Ah,” Earl said slowly. “Myrna’s maps are always outdated.”

        “Outdated? How often does a waterfall change?”

        “Well look what we have here, Earl,” Bertha said as a tree shook back and forth. “We’ve got a regular ‘ole expert on waterfalls.”

        Benjamin blushed lightly. He had never heard of a waterfall that changed directions or moved or relocated. But then again, he never heard of talking trees.

        “I’m just thinking that I’ve got this map and that’s the only way I know how to get home.”

        “Where are you from?” asked Bertha.

        “New York.”

        The trees shook violently back and forth and then didn’t say a word.

        “Anyways,” Benjamin said as he sighed in frustration. “Can you help me find the exit to the forest that’s opposite the desert?”

        “You came through the desert?” Gertrude asked. “No one survives the desert. That’s one of the most dangerous places outside of all the castles.”

        Benjamin felt a little proud of himself because he did, indeed, survive. It wasn’t so bad, he thought. He could do it again if he needed to.

        “If you need to exit opposite the desert, you’re going to need to head for the canyon. The canyon will take you to a river and then Myrna’s map may be able to guide you the rest of the way,” Earl said.

        “Great,” Benjamin said and then paused for a moment. “But, which way is the canyon.”

        “I’m pointing to it,” Earl said.

        Benjamin looked up at the nearest tree and saw a trunk soaring into the sky with hundreds of branches pointing every possible direction. He was a little embarrassed because he did not know what Earl meant.

        “I’m sorry but I can’t--”

        “Can’t you see, Benjamin?” Bertha asked with somewhat concern. “Earl is pointing in the direction of the canyon.”

        “It’s right there, Benjamin,” Earl said.

        “I can see but it’s just that there’s so many--”

        “He can’t see,” Gertrude said.

        “I can see,” Benjamin responded. “But your branches are going in so--”

        “There!” Earl said.

        “Right there!” Bertha echoed.

        Benjamin looked into the sky and decided he could only know which direction to go if he started to walk. He took three steps forward in one direction.

        “No!” Earl yelled.

        Then, he stopped and took three steps to his left.

        “No!” Bertha yelled.

        Then, he stopped and took three steps backwards.

        “Ah, almost but!” Earl yelled.

        Before Earl or Bertha could say anything more, Benjamin turned a little bit to the right and started to walk.

        “That’s it!” Gertrude said. “I guess he can see.”

        “So if I walk straight, I’ll walk to the canyon?” Benjamin asked the trees.

        “You won’t able to miss it,” Bertha said.

        “And if you need directions in the future,” Earl continued. “Just ask someone. It’s incredibly rude to smash red berries into our sides. Unless you see James. You can smash red berries in the side of James. He’s the worst.”

        “Oh yes,” Bertha said. “James is the worst.”

        “The worst indeed,” Gertrude echoed.

        “OK,” Benjamin said as he started to walk forward. “Thanks for your help and sorry again about your tree trunks.

        “Yeah, yeah,” said Earl. “Get out of here you little punk.”

        Benjamin placed one foot in front of the other and found a spot as far in the distance as he could see. He would walk as straight as he could because, he decided, he was not about to ask any more trees for help. And, understandably, he was not about to mark his path, either.

Chapter 21: In Which There Is A Request

        By his own calculation, Benjamin had taken three thousand steps before he reached the edge of the forest. And as far as he could tell, these steps had been in the same direction as each other because he saw a canyon extend into the horizon. The sun was setting now that he left the forest and Benjamin decided it was as good a time as any to get some rest.

        He found a tree that looked as comfortable as any to rest against and closed his eyes as the crisp warm air brushed against his clothes. They had been closed for only minutes when he heard a familiar sound.

        “South! South!” the squirrel yelled as it ran by. “South! South!”

        Benjamin watched the little creature run by his side and out of sight, leaving a small dust trail in his path. With the disturbance having passed, Benjamin once again closed his eyes to relax in the sun. And just as he was settled, the voice appeared again.

        “North! North!” the squirrel yelled as it ran by again. “North! North!”

        Benjamin jumped to his feet and took one step toward the squirrel who ran away faster. The little woodland creature was running back and forth. Only seconds later, Benjamin saw the squirrel turn around in the distance and begin running back again.

        “South! South!” the squirrel yelled in the distance.

        Realizing that his nap time had passed and that this squirrel was going to run back and forth indefinitely, Benjamin positioned himself directly in the path of the animal.

        “South! South!” the squirrel screamed.

        Benjamin shifted to his left and then to his right, making sure the squirrel was going to run straight at him.

        “South! South!” the squirrel screamed as its eyes widened and slobber flew from its lips.

        It was only twenty feet away when Benjamin stretched his arms as wide as they could reach.

        “South! South!”

        Benjamin braced himself for a collision with a little animal weighing little more than an orange.

        “SOUTH!”

        As the squirrel neared, it did not slow. Benjamin crouched to the ground and lunged at the little guy. And although Benjamin thought he would quickly outmatch the tiny speed demon, it was going so fast that it hit Benjamin square in the stomach. The young boy and the adrenaline-filled squirrel rolled together over and over and over until both of them lay side by side with their arms stretched to their sides and their legs in the air.

        “South,” the squirrel said faintly.

        “Ouch,” said Benjamin.

        Neither animal moved for a minute as they both pondered what just happened.

        “My head hurts,” the squirrel whispered as it stood up and adjusted the goggles it had been wearing to keep dirt out of its eyes.

        “You ran right into me,” Benjamin said as he rubbed his belly. “Your head should hurt.”

        “I didn’t even see you.”

        “I was standing right in front of you the entire time. How didn’t you see me?”

        “I run with my eyes closed.”

        “You run with your eyes closed? How do you know where you’re going?”

        “I yell the direction I want to go. If I want to go north, I yell north! If I want to go south, I yell south. East? East! West? West!”

        “But yelling the direction isn’t going to help when you run into something.”

        “I’ve been running around for forty years. I’ve learned a thing or two. First thing I learned is that yes, you do run into things. Second thing I learned is that I can run a lot faster if I run with my eyes closed.”

        “Where are you running to?”

        “Well, that’s a better question than asking about the weather,” the squirrel said as it propped its elbow on its knee and tilted its head to the sky. It tapped the ground several times with its tail and then smacked its lips. “I supposed you can ask where are we all running? Or, are we running? Or, are we running to something or are we running from something.” The squirrel got to his feet and pressed its finger against its forehead. It stopped and shifted its head back quickly. “Or, we’re running from and to something! But what does that mean while we’re running? I suppose that would mean we’re always running from something and we’re always running to something.”

        “But you were just running,” Benjamin said. “What were you running from?”

        “Bears.”

        “Bears?”

        “Oh, yes. The bears. They’re awful. Will eat you alive, really. Scary things. That’s another reason I close my eyes: bears. If I can’t see the bears, I won’t be scared of them.”

        “You could avoid them if you saw them.”

        The squirrel paced back and forth.

        “I suppose you’re right. If I saw the bears, I could avoid them.”

        “So you are running away from bears?”

        “Well, I’m running away from a lot of issues,” the squirrel said as he nudged Benjamin’s sneaker with its elbow. “But yes, mostly bears. And, my fear of failure but we don’t need to talk about that. How did you get so smart?”

        “I don’t think I’m very smart.”

        “Well, you certainly ask interesting questions. I’ve been running back and forth away from bears for forty years and no one has asked me these types of questions.”

        “I suppose I’m curious.”

        “Curiosity is a great thing!” the squirrel said quickly. “Don’t ever lose that. Curiosity can get you very far.”

        “I’ll remember that,” Benjamin said.

        “Say,” the squirrel said as it wiped the dirt from its goggles. “You’re actually smart and curious enough to help one of my friends.”

        “What kind of help does he need?”

        “Well, you see he collects nuts. Walnuts, to be precise. And, you see he’s got this problem where he can’t chew very well anymore. It’s embarrassing and pretty gross and all. So he’s got all these walnuts but he can’t eat them because he can’t get the shell open. Do you think you could help him?”

        “He needs someone to open the walnuts for him?”

        “Yes, you see he can’t do it himself. I don’t know him very well but I’m sure he’d pay you back.”
        Benjamin thought that this was going to distract him from his plan and although he wouldn’t mind meeting another talking creature, he just couldn’t. It would be a distraction.

        “I wish I had time to help your friend, but I really must get going,” Benjamin said to the sad squirrel.

        The squirrel lowered his head slowly and began twisting his left foot into the ground.

        “Oh,” the squirrel said slowly. “I understand. It’s OK, really. He can starve.”

        “I don’t want him to starve,” Benjamin pleaded.

        “Not all animals have to eat, right? He should be fine eating... air.”

        “No, he’s not going to be able to eat air.”

        “It’s OK,” the squirrel said as it waved its hand at Benjamin and began to take one step away. “You go wherever you need to go. Chesterfield will just have to develop a taste for eating dirt.”

        Benjamin felt bad. He knew that he could easily open walnuts and he didn’t want this Chesterfield person to starve. He took one deep breath and wondered how much time it would take to crack a couple walnuts. If there were ten, it might take him less than ten minutes. He didn’t know exactly but he was fine to take ten minutes to feed another animal.

        “Alright,” Benjamin said. “Where are these walnuts?”
        The squirrel jumped in the air and shook Benjamin’s hand with a new energy Benjamin had not yet see in the animal. And as soon as Benjamin realized that the squirrel had not yet let got of his hand, it ran as fast as it could, pulling Benjamin along behind it.

        “North! North!” the squirrel yelled as it ran faster and faster. Benjamin’s body was bouncing up and down on the grass, creating one of the biggest grass stains ever seen. “North! North!”

        When they arrived at a large tree in the middle of a meadow, Benjamin looked behind him to see the path that his body had created by being dragged on the ground. It was exhausting and his body ached. The squirrel, however, had not lost his enthusiasm for Benjamin’s willingness to help.

        The squirrel ran to the trunk and knocked loudly. Benjamin heard another creature running around inside the massive tree. It was as tall as it was wide and had thousands of branches, each branch with thousands of leaves. It stood alone in a meadow a couple miles wide in every direction with mountains lining the edges. Wind brushed through wheat that had grown to Benjamin’s waist as lonely cloud after lonely cloud took turns blocking the sun.

        The squirrel knocked loudly on the tree.

        “Chesterfield!” the squirrel said. “I found a problem to all of our, I mean your problems! It’s time to eat, Chesterfield!”

        The running inside the tree came to an abrupt stop. The squirrel took a couple steps back from the trunk and a previously invisible door opened slowly.

        “What do you want, Barnaby?” an unknown figure shouted from within the tree.

        “Chesterfield, I’ve found our hero!”

        “What did you do with your bird?”

        “It flew away,” Barnaby the squirrel said sadly.

        “It flew away?” Chesterfield asked with disbelief.

        “Yup, it flew away,” Barnaby said while averting his eyes to Barnaby.

        “It was dead.”

        “Yup, flew away and all,” Barnaby said as he acted out the flying of a bird with his hands.

        “I gave it to you as a present.”

        “Well, if you wanted me to keep it,” Barnaby protested. “Maybe you shouldn’t hand out presents that are dead birds.”

        “Excuse me,” Benjamin said. “I don’t have much time. Can you give me whatever walnuts you have to open?”

        The door to the tree trunk opened completely, a three foot by three foot door within the frame of a tree ten times that width. And standing within this relatively large door was a relatively small chipmunk named Chesterfield. He stood not four inches tall with one of the worst smiles Benjamin had seen on any living thing. If apples were smiles, Chesterfield’s smile was a rotten apple smashed under truck and then scooped up with a shovel. It was difficult for Benjamin to look directly in his face.

        “Why that’s wonderful!” Chesterfield exclaimed with his arms stretched to his sides. “Barnaby, you should have told me.”

        “I did tell you. This child is going to be our hero.”

        “Let me get the walnuts!”

        Chesterfield slammed the door closed and ran throughout the tree, shaking a branch and some leaves here and there. Benjamin heard the sounds of musical instruments, construction, and loud engines throughout the tree as Chesterfield moved about. It was the biggest house for a chipmunk Benjamin had ever seen. When the door swung open again minutes later, Chesterfield appeared with five walnuts in his mouth and hands. He scooted across the grass and placed them at Benjamin’s feet.

        Benjamin couldn’t believe his luck. There were only five walnuts. This would take him no time at all. He would help a starving chipmunk and be on his way in a matter of minutes.

        He picked up the walnuts and examined each to see that they were ordinary walnuts that he had seen in his mother’s kitchen many times. He knew how to open these shells because he had seen his parents open them many times before. All he had to do was find something hard to slam them against. And having learned his lesson in the forest, he wasn’t about to slam the walnuts into the side of the tree. He strolled around the meadow and searched for a large rock, something the size of his head that would be sturdy enough with a flat surface to absorb a walnut.

        He placed a rock between Barnaby and Chesterfield as they watched Benjamin’s every move with increasing interest.

        “Here we go,” Benjamin said.

        Chesterfield put his hand against the rock. It looked like a man standing next to a huge boulder.

        “Do you know how long it took us to move that rock from here to where you just picked it up?” Chesterfield said with annoyance.

        “I’ll put it back!” Benjamin said.

        Chesterfield and Barnaby stood to the side. Benjamin picked up a walnut and slammed it against the rock, creating a quick cracking noise with a sudden burst of shell in the air. The squirrel and chipmunk covered their heads as it began to rain shell fragments.

        Benjamin picked up the second. Crack!

        The third. Crack!

        The fourth. Crack!

        And, the fifth. Crack!

        He had opened all of the walnuts in less than five minutes. And, he felt really happy that he had done something that he had only seen his parents do. Now he knew he could do it as well.

        The tiny little jaws of Chesterfield and Barnaby nearly touched the ground. They shook their heads and then applauded as an audience would applaud a golfer’s drive.

        “That was,” Chesterfield said, taking a slow step forward, “extremely impressive.”

        “Thanks,” Benjamin said, happy to receive the compliment. “And, now you can eat!”

        “Yes,” Barnaby said, taking a quick step forward. “Now, he can eat!”

        “So, I should get going but I’m glad I was able to help you,” Benjamin said. “Enjoy the walnuts.”

        Benjamin grabbed his backpack and examined the long grass stain along his shirt. It would probably come out in the wash, he thought. They always did. As he started to walk away, Barnaby ran in front of Benjamin and put his hands out.

        “Actually,” Barnaby said. “We didn’t realize you would be so good at opening walnuts. Five walnuts is great. Don’t get us wrong. We are very grateful for your help. But if we had a few more walnuts open, then Chesterfield could eat all month without having to find someone else to open them.”

        “Barnaby, I must be going. Why don’t you try opening the walnuts?”

        Barnaby held his fingers forward and Benjamin saw that they had all been worn out, presumably from opening many walnuts.

        “How many more walnuts?” Benjamin asked. He couldn’t stay long but he could open a couple more. And, it was a lot of fun smashed walnuts into a rock.

        “Not many. As many as you want to do.”

        “OK, bring out the walnuts.”

        Benjamin returned to the rock and waited for Barnaby to stop whispering to Chesterfield so that he could begin opening the nuts.

        “Actually,” Chesterfield said. “Would you mind coming inside my treehouse to open the rest?”

        “Inside the treehouse? Why?”

        “Well, I thought I could offer you a spot of tea and some biscuits.”

        Benjamin only thought briefly about what biscuits and tea a chipmunk was capable of making.

        “I am hungry,” Benjamin said. “Sure.”

        Benjamin was also curious to see the inside of the tree. After he watched Chesterfield walk inside, he stuck his own head in to look inside. The space was as high as the tree and along the cylindrical room was a spiral staircase that spun around shelves and shelves of walnuts.

        “Come on in!” Barnaby said.

        “Yes, come in,” Chesterfield said as he pulled out some biscuits and tea. Barnaby and Chesterfield looked at the size of the biscuits and then compared them to the size of Benjamin. They were not nearly as large as they should be to satisfy the child’s appetite. “Come in! Come in!”

        Benjamin squeezed through the tiny door until he was completely inside the tree. Barnaby shut the door and Chesterfield handed Benjamin biscuit after biscuit. As Benjamin ate the delicious treats, he began to wonder why there were so many walnuts on the shelves.

        “So you really like walnuts?” Benjamin said.

        “I don’t know if like is the right word, but we’ve gotten used to them,” Chesterfield said.

        “It looks like there are hundreds here,” Benjamin replied as he sipped tea and ate another biscuit. He had eaten nearly a dozen so far while Barnaby was halfway through his first biscuit.

        “Well,” Chesterfield said as he tapped his fingers together. “There are actually a few more than that.”

        “A few more?”

        “Not many more. Just a few. We were hoping you could help us open a few. You opened the five walnuts so quickly that it will take you such a short amount of time than it would take us.”

        “How many is a few?” Benjamin asked with caution.

        Barnaby and Chesterfield started to ascend the staircase backwards.

        “Excuse me,” Benjamin said as he noticed the chipmunk and squirrel walking up the staircase. “How many is a few?”

        They kept walking faster and faster and faster until they were out of Benjamin’s reach.

        “Forty-two million,” Barnaby said.

        “Forty-two million walnuts!” Benjamin explained as he stood on his feet. “I am not going to open forty-two million walnuts!”

        “It’ll take you such a short amount of time compared to how long it will take us!” Barnaby protested.

        “But, I’m not concerned with how long it would take you. It’s going to take me longer than I offered you.”

        “But we gave you biscuits.”

        “I’ll open a dozen and then that’s it,” Benjamin decided. He grabbed twelve walnuts from the shelf and then smashed them individually on the stone inside the tree. Twelve open walnuts appeared on the ground below and as before, Chesterfield and Barnaby stood on the staircase and golf clapped. “Now, I must be leaving.”

        Benjamin gathered his backpack and went to open the door from which he entered. But, it was locked. He pushed on it as hard as he could but it would not open. There wasn’t anything that would allow him to unlock the door.

        “Hey!” Benjamin yelled up the tree house. “The door is locked.”

        “Yes,” Chesterfield said. “It is.”

        “I would like you to open it for me.”

        “I don’t think we can do that.”

        “Why not?”

        “Because we have a lot of walnuts that need to be opened.”

        “You open this door right now!”

        “If you open all of the walnuts, we will open it immediately.”

        Benjamin was irate. He shook the door violently which caused the tree to vibrate ever so slightly. Walnuts fell from the tallest of shelves. He grumbled and his voiced echoed throughout the tree. Barnaby and Chesterfield climbed higher and higher.

        “Once you open all the walnuts,” Chesterfield said. “We will unlock the door.”

        It would take days, weeks, months, and maybe a year to open forty-two million walnuts. There was no chance he could do this within a reasonable amount of time. But Benjamin realized something else which was much more important to him. He had been tricked. He assumed he would only have to open a few more walnuts, not millions. And now that he was locked inside the tree trunk, he was being held prisoner by a chipmunk and a squirrel.

        He decided not to open a single walnut.

        “I’m not opening any!” Benjamin yelled.

        “Fine, fine,” Chesterfield said. “But if you don’t any, then you’ll probably starve unless you can eat air. You’re going to have to open a few to satisfy yourself. Those biscuits will only last so long. If you need us, we’ll be in the loft.”

        Barnaby and Chesterfield ran all the way up the staircase to the top of the tree to a loft that was away from Benjamin’s sight. The staircase was too narrow and thin to support Benjamin’s weight. It would break on the first step.

        Benjamin needed to figure out how to leave. He couldn’t open the door and he wasn’t going to open all the walnuts.

        He paced back and forth at the bottom of the tree trunk thinking about all the ways that one might be able to leave.

        He could dig a tunnel. But, he did not have a shovel and the trunk was too tough for his hands.

        He could punch a hole in the tree trunk. But, the tree trunk was more than a foot thick and he didn’t have anything that strong.

        He could explode the entire tree with dynamite. But, he didn’t have any dynamite.

        And then he settled upon a solution that was the least easy but the most promising. He would scale the inside of the tree like a rock climber scaling the side of a mountain. At the top, he would grab Barnaby and Chesterfield.

        Benjamin put his backpack on and found the wall that he would like to scale. He had never climbed any wall or shelf and this one, unfortunately, was very, very high.

        He sighed with frustration at being trapped in the tree. And as soon as he took a breath, he began his climb. Hand over hand and foot over foot he climbed and climbed. Walnuts feel everywhere as he shook the shelves and moved his feet and hands in different spots. It was difficult but Benjamin was beginning to get comfortable with what he was doing.

        He was comfortable until he looked down.

        He was twenty feet above the ground and realized that he had nothing to stop him from falling on the ground.

        But, it was too late. He was nearly halfway to the top of the tree and he needed to continue. With each climb, he knocked walnuts to the ground. His left foot slipped off as he tried to find a sturdy location to place it, causing him to hold on as tightly as he could with just his hands. The walnuts falling to the ground sounded like a pitter patter of rain against wood. Benjamin regained his composure by finding a resting spot for his left leg. He kept looking above so that he knew where he was going to step next.

Eventually, he made it to the top. And at the top, he saw a loft with a squirrel and a chipmunk asleep in front of a massive television screen. They had been watching some Spanish soap opera show together. Benjamin couldn’t believe that they were sleeping while forcing him to do all that work.

He squeezed into the loft and then slowly nudged his way behind them. When he decided that he was close enough and that they were still sleeping, he grabbed both of them at the same time.

“What! Woah! No!” Barnaby yelled.

“What is the meaning of this! Stop it!” Chesterfield screamed.

“That was not very nice!” Benjamin yelled at Barnaby and Chesterfield outside the tree.

        “We’re sorry! We’re sorry!” Barnaby said.

        “Yes, we’re terribly sorry!” Chesterfield said.

        “Let us go!”

        “You can go! We’ll set you free!”

        And then, unexpectedly, both of them burst into tears. The noises they made while crying were embarrassing. They cried and bawled and shook and screamed while Benjamin sat with Barnaby in his left hand and Chesterfield in his right. It was an uncomfortable situation.

        “We’re sorry!”

        “We know we’re awful!”

        “Don’t kill us!”

        “So sorry!”

        “Take all the walnuts you want. Have them all,” Barnaby said in tears.

        “Yes, you can have them all. Whatever you want. We’re really sorry.”

        “I don’t want any walnuts. I just want to leave,” Benjamin said without letting go of either animal.

        “Fine, fine, we’ll let you go. Don’t kill us! Please don’t kill us.”

        “I’m not going to kill you!” Benjamin said.

        “You’re not?” Barnaby asked.

        “No, why would you think I’m going to kill you?”

        “Because that’s what Aloysius promises to do whenever he grabs us.”

        “Aloysius Trenhaile?” Benjamin asked with curiosity. “How do you know him?”

        “Little boy,” Chesterfield said. “Why do you think we have forty-two million walnuts?”

        “So, you don’t starve.”

        “No, that’s not exactly it,” said Barnaby. “I may have lied a bit.”

        “You both lied a lot!”

        “Right,” Barnaby said. “We lied a lot. We send walnuts to Aloysius Trenhaile’s castle for food.”

        “You send him food? Why?”

        “So that he doesn’t kill us,” Chesterfield said. “We put all of the cracked walnuts in that basket on the branch over there.” Chesterfield pointed to a basket on the opposite wall from the loft. “Every week, a bird comes and picks it up to take it to Aloysius Trenhaile. And in exchange, we get to live another week.”

        “That doesn’t sound like a great way to live.”

        “Right!” Barnaby said. “That’s why we’ve been trying to find someone else to open up all these walnuts. So, we don’t have to!”

        “That doesn’t make what you did to me right.”

        “We know!” Barnaby and Chesterfield yelled together.

        And for the second time, they cried and bawled and shook and screamed as Benjamin’s grip on the both of them remained tight.

        Benjamin looked at all of the walnuts and then looked at the basket. That basket was headed for Aloysius Trenhaile’s castle and that was where Benjamin was.

        “When does the bird come next?” Benjamin asked the two of them.

        “This afternoon,” Chesterfield said. “That basket is ready to go.”

        “OK,” Benjamin said. “I’m going to hide underneath the walnuts in that basket and have that bird take me to that castle.”

        “You could do that if you want, or we could just open the door for you to leave,” Barnaby offered.

        “I have to go to Aloysius Trenhaile’s Castle and that bird will be able to take me there.

        “OK,” Chesterfield said. “We can hide you in the basket.”

        “After the end of the movie?” Barnaby asked.

        “Fine,” Benjamin said as he tightened his grip on the two animals.

        Benjamin sat with a fist clenched around a chipmunk and a squirrel until the Spanish romance had concluded. Then, he slipped across to the other side of the tree and tunnelled his way through the walnuts to get to the bottom.

        “We’re really sorry again,” Chesterfield said.

        “It’s OK, but don’t do it to anyone else,” Benjamin said.

        “We won’t!” Barnaby said.

        “And, I’m sorry you have to work for Aloysius,” Benjamin said. “I hear bad things about him.”

        “Be very careful,” Chesterfield said. “Aloysius Trenhaile is the most dangerous creature alive.”

        Chesterfield and Barnaby then covered the basket with a blanket as Benjamin waited for the bird to arrive.

Chapter 22: In Which There Is A Bird

        Benjamin waited five minutes at the bottom of the basket before he felt it jostle back and forth. He wasn’t sure if it was Chesterfield and Barnaby so he started to push walnut after walnut away from his face. He did not want to startle whatever was making such loud thumping noises so he carefully peeled away a corner of the blanket that covered the basket. On the other side, Benjamin saw the ugliest, biggest, most vile goose he had ever seen.

        Before the goose could see him, Benjamin threw back the blanket and covered himself with walnuts again. The bird was nearly ten times larger than Benjamin, which explained why the basket was so large. It was also ten times more disgusting. It smelled like sewage and it kept burping all over the basket.

        This animal was far from graceful. And if its hygiene was related to its flying abilities, Benjamin feared for his safety. He wondered if this was a good idea. Surely, this was only one way to get to Aloysius Trenhaile’s castle. This didn’t have to be the way. He could go by land. It would certainly be safer than by air with this awful beast. It didn’t even have all of its feathers. And, some of the feathers that did remain were painted odd colors like orange and blue. They were certainly not feathers one would find on a goose.

        Benjamin wanted to leave.

        He moved the walnuts from his face and began to make his escape. The goose kept nudging the basket back and forth while making a loud chirping noise that ended in loud coughing. It had some sort of respiratory problem. As Benjamin peeled back the blanket, he started to climb from the basket while the goose looked the other direction. He pulled his weight as quickly as he could and lifted one leg over the basket. And as soon as he was about to lift the other leg over the basket, the goose began to flap its wings as strongly as it could. It created a vortex of stinky, awful wind that pushed Benjamin right back into the basket. The wind swirled around as a tornado would. Dust and dirt and awful smells blew about. Benjamin used all of his strength to get back to his feet and to step into the gusts pushing him back. He reached the ledge of the basket. And as soon as he jumped in the air to clear the basket and return to the ground, the goose grabbed the handle and lifted the basket into the sky.

        It went higher and Benjamin could see the top of the treehouse.

        It went even higher and Benjamin could see the expansive meadow.

        It went even higher still and Benjamin could see all the mountains that surrounded the meadow.

        And when it went so high that Benjamin could see the large forest from which he came, he knew he had no choice but to sit and enjoy the ride. Leaving the basket now would certainly mean death. He would have to endure the flight, as smelly and stinky as it was.

        Benjamin had never been this high above the ground in his life. He watched with amazement as a river carved its way between rolling green hills into a mountain range capped with white tops. A volcano in the distanced puffed smoke into the air next to a lake which glistened under the beaming sun. He could see meadows stretch for distances he could not imagine walking and canyons that he could not imagine traversing. The land extended in all directions and it became apparent that this land was not small.

        Nor, was it empty.

        Benjamin could see Castle Ehtahl, from which he recently left. And in the opposite direction, he could see nearly a dozen other castles of various shapes and designs. The only characteristic that they appeared to have in common was that all of the castles had very high walls.

        Except one.

        One castle in the far distance had very low walls. Unlike all of the others, however, it had fire on the ground as well as fire throughout the buildings. There was dark, black smoke billowing throughout and an occasional ball of flame shot into the air. It looked uninviting and deeply disturbing.

        And, that was exactly where this ugly goose was headed.

        Benjamin knew it must be the castle because it alone looked like a nightmare. It was the worst, most awful place Benjamin had ever seen or imagined in his life. It was both scary and terrifying. And, Benjamin did not want to go there. He looked to the ground and guessed that he was several thousand feet above the ground. The chances of him landing safely or alive were slim. He needed to get this goose to fly lower.

        Without creating too much of a plan, Benjamin grabbed a walnut and threw it at the goose. The filthy bird screamed in annoyance and shook the basket from side to side. This was not what Benjamin wanted. He needed another plan.

        As Benjamin thought hard, the goose shook the basket from side to side again. And then, it did it again. With each subsequent shaking, the goose moved the basket more and more violently.

        Benjamin looked ahead and noticed that objects were being fired passed the goose at an amazing speeds. One flew by and then another. And then, another. He looked in the direction from which they were coming and was able to see a group of people in a meadow.

        They were trying to shoot down the goose.

        The bird swung its wings left and nearly escaped getting hit. Then, it banked to the right and avoided several more shots being fired directly at it. Benjamin held onto the basket as tightly as he could, fearful that either the goose was going to drop him or that the bird would be hit.

        The goose jerked to the right suddenly and several walnuts flew from the basket to the ground below. Benjamin gripped the side as tightly as he could. The goose began to spiral to avoid the shots being fired at it.

        And then, it was hit.

        Something hit the goose square in the head. The bird, the basket, and the boy were in freefall headed straight for land. Feathers flew everywhere and the goose screamed in panic. Benjamin screamed too. He was crashing into the earth and this was not an enjoyable adventure.

        The air moved so fast that Benjamin could not tell how close he was to land. And since there was no sign of slowing down, he decided the best thing for him to do was close his eyes. He did not want to see what was going to happen next.

        He braced for impact.

        The goose braced for impact.

        And the basket splintered into a thousand pieces.

        Benjamin bounced into the air with a thousand walnuts around him.

        And when he landed, he skidded along a dirt path and fell neatly along a feathery pillow. The feathery pillow was, of course, the goose which broke Benjamin’s fall. After a few seconds, it knocked Benjamin away and investigated the mess of the situation. The basket was destroyed and walnuts were everywhere. And to the birds’ surprise, it had been hauling a person along too.

        It stretched its broken feathers as wide as it could and screeched at Benjamin as loudly as it could. Benjamin, however, was dazed from the fall and simply sat while the bird yelled and screamed at him. He could have cared less. He did not know how he survived such a landing.

        Before he could react to the goose, the bird gathered some walnuts in its mouth and fled. It flew above the canyon nearby and eventually, beyond Benjamin’s sight. Benjamin hoped to never see that animal again. Or, at the very least, to never smell it again.

        Benjamin would have continued to sit in a daze for a little longer but he heard footsteps and yelling from the other side of the hill that he had landed upon. He ran for his backpack, grabbed it, and then hid behind a boulder five times his size.

        The footsteps were louder and closer. Benjamin didn’t know how many people were running toward him but he guessed it was a dozen. He stayed as still as he could with his back to the boulder. And then, he saw a shadow cast beyond him. And then, another. And, another. There were more than a dozen people together.

        “Where’d it go?” said a voice.

        “Must’ve flown away,” said another.

        “It dropped the basket,” said another. “So, I guess we’re good.”

        “Alright, let’s take the basket and get back to camp,” said the first voice.

        Benjamin couldn’t see the people but he heard them gather the basket and pick up the walnuts. The shadows moved quickly and Benjamin’s curiosity grew. He leaned against the boulder and slowly started to move to its edge. He peeked around the side and saw lots of people dressed completely in blue.

        And among all the people dressed in blue, one stopped moving and stared straight at Benjamin. Benjamin froze. Dozens of people in blue moved frantically around the one person who stayed completely still.

        Benjamin ran.

        He ran as fast as he could through cacti and rocks.

        And as he ran, he heard footsteps chasing after him.

        He weaved every which way he could and heard the footsteps fading in the distance. He paused for a moment to look behind. There were only rocks and cacti everywhere. He sat down to catch his breath, his heart still racing.

        He didn’t know who those people were or why they were after the basket. There was a hill not too far away and Benjamin thought about climbing it in order to see if he could watch the people. He wondered where they were going.

        But as he continued to catch his breathe, he felt two hands on his back.

        “Don’t move,” said the voice. “Don’t move a muscle.”

        Benjamin didn’t move. He didn’t have a plan to escape.

        “Don’t move and do exactly as I say,” the voice continued.

        “OK. OK,” Benjamin said quickly. “What do you want?”

        “Don’t move!”

        “Did you shoot down the goose?”

        “Yep, I’m the one that hit it.”

        Benjamin recognized the voice and spun around to see Selma Applebaum standing before him. She wore a small blue outfit and had her arms crossed over her body. Her head shook side to side as she wore an expression of disbelief.

        “And, we didn’t know you would be inside the basket,” Selma Applebaum said. “What, Benjamin Buckingham, were you doing inside it?”

        She looked angry and Benjamin was worried she still had whatever it was that knocked down the huge bird.

        “I’m trying to leave,” Benjamin said.

        “You’re obviously not doing very well,” Selma said.

        “Well, of course not. I’m still here aren’t I?”

        “You were going to leave by goose?”

        “I was just along for the ride. It was going to take me to where I could get a key to leave.”

        “Do you have any idea where you were headed?”

        “I told you. I was going to get the key so that I could leave.”

        “I’m so confused by you, Benjamin. How do you know where the key is?”

        “Oh,” Benjamin said as he rushed to grab his backpack. He unfolded his dirty map and showed it to Selma. “Here. I got this map that shows me where it is.”

        Selma examined the map and scratched her head.

        “This isn’t a very good map,” she said. “So where were you trying to go? That goose was headed for an awful place. Were you trying to go there?”

        “Yes, I was headed to Aloysius Trenhaile’s castle. That’s where the goose was headed,” Benjamin said.

        Selma looked confused and Benjamin wasn’t sure why she was becoming more frustrated.

        “Keep quiet,” Selma said as she rushed him behind a boulder a little further away. “They’re going to realize I left so I have to return soon. Listen, you can’t go to Aloysius Trenaile’s castle by way of a goose. If you arrive in a basket full of walnuts, you’ll be killed. You’re lucky I was able to shoot down that bird. I saved your life.”

        “And, you also almost killed me in the process!”

        “The way I look at it is that you were doomed either way.”

        “What are you doing out here?”

        “I snuck out. I wanted to help destroy the nightmares.”

        “Destroy the nightmares? How does shooting a goose destroy nightmares?”

        “Benjamin, I’m surprised you’re still alive,” Selma said as she strained her neck to see if the others were still around. “The birds deliver food to Aloysius Trenhaile’s castle. We shoot down the birds to stop the food deliveries. Without food, they starve and can’t make nightmares. If you go to Aloysius Trenhaile’s castle by bird, you’re going to get shot down again. At least, that’s our goal.”

“Then, how do I get to the castle? I need King Bernard Zwirn to give me his key.”

Selma paused as they listened to the footsteps of the others beginning to leave. She grabbed Benjamin by the shoulders and stared straight into his face.

“Meet me tomorrow outside Castle Ehtsetra,” she said quickly.

“Where is that?”

Selma grabbed Benjamin’s map and scribbled a path through it.

“You’re here right now on the map and this is where I’ll meet you. See you tomorrow,” she said as she sprinted back to her group.

“But why--”

“Tomorrow!” Selma yelled.

Chapter 23: In Which There Is A Glass Castle

        Benjamin waited at the front of the gate for Selma to appear. He spent the night traversing a river, climbing the side of a cliff, hiking through fields of tangled weeds, and sleeping in a cave. He was bruised and cut and very, very sleepy. And as he waited, he wondered how long he could continue like this. It appeared to him that his exit was further and further away.

        “What happened to you?” Selma asked as she approached Benjamin.

        “What do you mean? I’ve been living outside for the past few days. This is what you get after you’ve been living outside for a while,” Benjamin responded.

        “You should at least have taken a shower?”

        “Where?” Benjamin held his arms out to present the wild environment around him.

        “Where is your map?” Selma asked. “There are public restrooms everywhere.”

        “In my backpack,” Benjamin’s jaw hung low. He had no idea there might be public restrooms here.

        Before Benjamin had a chance to retrieve the map for Selma, she snatched the backpack away and rummaged through it. She took out a small notebook, which she thought contained the map. But when she looked at the cover, she realized it was something completely different.

        “What is this?” Selma asked.

        “It’s nothing,” Benjamin said as he took the backpack and extracted the map. “Here’s what you want.”

        “No, this is much more interesting. Is this a book of your dreams?”

        “No, it’s nothing. Take the map.”

        Selma scurried away with the notebook as Benjamin chased quickly after her.

        “Oh,” Selma said as she paged through it. “It’s a list of your nightmares.”

        Selma stopped suddenly and turned to face Benjamin.

        “This isn’t a book of your dreams,” she said with surprise. “This is a book of nightmares. This book is full of nightmares. Why have you had so many nightmares?”

        Benjamin stopped and took the book away from Selma. He shoved it into his backpack and walked away.

        “Wait, don’t leave so quickly,” Selma said as she chased after him. “Why do you have so many nightmares? There are more nightmares in that book than I knew existed. I mean I knew there was a nightmare problem but you’ve got a really bad nightmare problem. Why do you have so many?”

        “I don’t know. Wouldn’t you know better than I would?”

        “Well, I don’t think I would. I’ve always been told that nightmares target people who are susceptible to them.”

        “What does that mean?”

        “It means there’s something in your life that makes you likely to have nightmares.”

        “Like what?”

        “That’s what I’m asking you. What’s so awful about your life that you’ve got all these nightmares?”

        “My life’s good.”

        “Good family?”

        “Yep.”

        “You eat well?”

        “Sure.”

        “And you exercise?”

        “Yeah.”

        “And you get enough sleep?”

        “Yep.”

        “And you’re healthy?”

        “I get sick once in awhile.”

        “But not all the time?”

        “No, not all the time.”

        “And you’ve got a bunch of friends?”

        “Listen, are we going to go inside the castle or not?”

        “Yeah, we’ll go in the castle but why didn’t you answer my question?”

        “Because you’re being really annoying.”

        “It’s not like it’s a complicated question.”

        “Stop!”

        Selma paused and watched Benjamin march away quickly. She followed him at a speedy trot.

        “So,” she said slowly. “Why don’t you have friends?”

        “I do have friends.”

        “Rodney Cohen?”

        “No, Snark.”

        “Do you have any friends your own age?”

        “Who needs friends? It’s not necessary.”

        “Everyone needs friends.”

        “I don’t.”

        “But, that might be why you have so many nightmares.”

        “I doubt it. It must be something else.”

        “You don’t like people?”

        “I like people just fine.”

        “Then why don’t you have friends? It seems to me like you should have at least a couple friends.”

        “Stop!”

        “OK,” Selma said. “I’ll stop. But, I don’t understand why you don’t have friends.”

        “Because people don’t like me.”

        “They don’t like you?”

        “No, they don’t like me.”

        “Why not?”

        “I don’t know. If I knew, I’d change it.”

        “Maybe you can’t change it.”

        “What do you mean?”

        “Maybe there’s something about you that other people just don’t like. Maybe it’s something you can’t change.”

        “Well then I won’t ever have friends.”

        “Probably not,” Selma said. “Unless, you make friends with people who like you for who you are.”

        “We’ve been talking about this for too long. I want to go in the castle.”

        Selma shrugged her shoulders and let out a loud sigh.

        “Fine, let’s go.”

        Selma and Benjamin approached a large glass drawbridge that was much thicker and taller than the one at Castle Ehtahl. The drawbridge connected the castle over a moat filled with chipped red stone that followed the walls wherever they went. The walls, too, were a blue glass that offered a slight view into what was hidden behind. And everything behind was made of glass as well, glass of varying colors. A figure moved back and forth on the other side of the drawbridge, pacing like a guard would but moving quickly like someone in a panic.

        On Benjamin’s side of the moat, he saw a large blue button that he assumed might be a doorbell. He moved his hand to press it because that’s what he does whenever he sees a button. But then, Selma grabbed his hand and gave him an unsatisfied look.

        “There’s a certain way that you push the doorbell,” she said. “Each different way you push the doorbell tells the guard on the other side where you come from. And if you don’t push the doorbell in any kind of recognizable way, he’ll assume you’re a nightmare or some other evil thing.”

        “How do you push the doorbell?” Benjamin asked curiously.

        “Well, we are going to act as delivery people,” Selma said as she pointed to the boxes that she brought with her. “So, it’ll be tap, tap, long, tap, long, tap.”

        “So these are secret passwords?”

        “Not really,” Selma said as she approached the doorbell. “It’s just that the evil can’t be bothered to slow down and think about anything for a little bit. They know the patterns. Evil is just always in a hurry for whatever reason.”

        Benjamin watched Selma tap the button, tap it again, press it for a long time, and then tap, long, and tap. The guard slightly visible through the glass stopped marching back and forth and appeared to put his hands on the glass drawbridge to try to see who was on the other side. A muffled sound appeared from around the blue button that seemed unintelligible at first.

        “Who goes there?” said a man, presumably the guard from the other side.

        Without pausing for a moment, Selma answered the voice.

        “Delivery!” she said.

        “Delivery? There are no deliveries on Saturdays.”

        “It’s a special delivery.”

        “What are you delivering?”

        “We are delivering boxes.”

        “We have plenty of boxes, thank you.”

        “Boxes filled with travel brochures.”

        “Travel brochures? But, why would anyone want to leave Castle Ehtsetra?”

        “They were requested to be printed by the local travel agency.”

        “And they asked for a Saturday delivery? This is quite peculiar.”

        “We’re a few days late. It should have been Thursday but we had to print a few more copies.”

        “How many copies do you have?”

        “Thousands.”

        “Thousands! Thousands of people want to travel?”

        “No, but thousands of people are interested in travelling.”

        “We have fiction books to satisfy those only interested in travelling.”

        “But these brochures are going to help inspire the writers of those books.”

        “I’ve tried writing a book once and and it’s an awful affair. Really, it’s a lonely struggle. I don’t know how one does it. If your brochures are going to help more books be written, I suppose that’s a good thing. Take a few steps back and I’ll lower the drawbridge. It’s made of glass so be mindful of any sharp edges.”

        The drawbridge jolted forward and then slowly began leaning forward as smoothly and gracefully as a massive sheet of glass could move. It glimmered in the sun and reflected different colors of blue in all directions. When the glass bridge finally stopped, Benjamin had his first glance behind the castle walls. And everything that he saw was made of glass. There were glass roads and glass trees and glass chairs and glass cars. There were glass trains and glass skyscrapers and glass clothing worn by otherwise normal looking people. It was nearly ten times as populous as Castle Ehtahl and everyone wore sunglasses, because among all of the glass were reflecting the sun. One skyscraper in particular appeared to soar so high into the blue sky so as to touch the sun in the distance.

        “That’s where we’re headed,” Selma said as she watched Benjamin’s eyes look higher and higher.

        As they walked across the bridge, the guard greeted them on the other side. He was nearly four times taller than he was wide, a peculiar difference from the guards at Castle Ehtahl. His white clothing took on different colors as he moved among the different colors of glass. Unlike Castle Ehtahl, he seemed quite professional and quite capable of being a guard.

        “You’re much smaller than I expected you to be,” the guard said to Selma and Benjamin as they stood inside the castle walls. “How old are you?”

        “We’re eight.”

        “Eight! Our eight year olds are nearly twice the size of you. Where are you from? Castle Eehjee? Castle Ayee? Castle Ihbihd? Castle Ahem? Castle Peehem?”

        Benjamin didn’t realize there were so many castles to come from. He thought it was like the number of cities to come from at home.

        “Castle Ehtahl.”

        “Oh, that was my next guess! That makes sense. You’re a bit shorter over there. Anyways, do you know where the travel agency is?”

        “Yep,” Selma said quickly. “We’ve made lots of deliveries before.”

        “It really troubles me that so many people in the castle would be wanting to travel. We have everything we need in Castle Ehtsetra. Why would anyone want to leave?” the guard responded.

        “I don’t know,” Selma said. “Maybe they want to see what exists somewhere else?”

        “That’s a bunch of rubbish in my mind. If they want to see what exists somewhere else, they should just read books.”

        “Well, maybe they could read books and travel.”

        “I’m not sure I like that. I think people should stay put. They should either be on one side of the wall or on the other side of the wall. I’ve been a guard for fifty years and let me tell you, it’s very hard to sit on a wall.”

        “So, we’re going to go,” Selma said as she started walking forward.

        “Fine,” the guard said. “But, don’t stay too long. You’re allowed to stay overnight but then you have to leave.”

        “Right, we’re not going to be long.”

        Selma and Benjamin walked among the hundreds of people moving to and fro in the middle of the glass city. They all had expressionless faces, their eyes hidden by dark sunglasses. Many people carried glass briefcases and just about everyone was wearing white clothing except for the occasional person who wore a blue color as blue as the sky.

        “Do you really come here often?” Benjamin asked Selma.

        “No,” she said quickly as she scanned the scene. “I’ve only been here once before when I was little.”

        “So, how do you know where we’re supposed to go?”

        “I’ve got a good memory,” she said. “Nothing much changes in Castle Ehtsetra. The glass doesn’t break as often as you’d think it would. We have to go up there.”

        Selma pointed to the top of the tall skyscraper Benjamin saw on the outside. It reached so far into the sky that it looked as if it might at one point leave the atmosphere.

        “What is up there?”

        “That’s where we find a way to get you back to your home, without having to go to Aloysius Trenhaile’s castle”

        They walked to an elevated tram station and joined the lines of people waiting for the next train. When it arrived, it was made of nothing but bright green glass that slid across an orange railway. They rode the train for a half an hour, moving throughout the city and stopping at various elevated train stations along the way. The glass structures allowed Benjamin to see into buildings in a way he could not have imagined when the walls were made of steel or brick. It allowed him to see people on different levels, interacting with their surroundings as if they were walking on air. The various colors of glass created different colors when seen through each other. And for all of the transparency throughout the buildings and structures and benches and greenery, the people were as quiet and still as possible. No one uttered a word to anyone else and no one seemed capable of a smile. They all looked like cloned models of each other, walking around a pristine glass environment.

        “How--” Benjamin began to ask but stopped. As soon as he opened his mouth, he saw every face in the train turn in his direction. He did not utter a word further but his first word continued to echo over and over. Selma looked at him and put her finger to her lips.

Benjamin and Selma exited at the station that led them to the skyscraper at the center of the town. They encountered hundreds of other people before entering the building. Once inside, Benjamin felt more comfortable speaking.

“No one says anything here,” he said to Selma. “How do people communicate if they cannot speak to one another?”

“Did you hear that echo when you said one word?” she responded. “Imagine if everyone were to talk at once. Our ears would explode with the noise.”

“So no one talks?”

“They do. I’ve seen them. But, they’re very careful with what they say because sounds bounce off the glass loudly.”

“Seems like a difficult way to live,” Benjamin whispered to Selma as they walked to the front desk.

“Or it’s as difficult as living in a place where everyone can say whatever they want but no one hears it.”

“I guess that’s the same thing,” Benjamin said as he looked upwards and wondered how many floors he could see through from the ground.

At the front desk, a woman wearing white with large, bushy red hair moved her head from side to side in a jerky motion. Benjamin wondered how she did not hurt her neck because he knew he could not do so without hurting himself.

“Hello, may I help you?” the lady asked with a gentle, bouncy tone.

“Yes,” Selma responded. “What floor is the travel agency on?”

As she asked for the travel agency, everyone on the floor around them stopped and looked in her direction. The lady at the front desk stopped moving her head from side to side and stared at Selma with a surprised expression. Benjamin could hear his heartbeat among the silence of everything around him.

“My dear,” the lady asked as she signaled to everyone else in the lobby to carry along. “Why do you want to go to the travel agency?”

“I have these boxes to deliver,” Selma said as she pointed to the boxes that Benjamin was carrying.

“Oh!” the lady responded. “That’s great! I thought for a second that you wanted to leave!”

        The lady laughed with a sound Benjamin thought was much louder than the woman was capable of making. She heaved her head back and forth as she pounded her fist into the table. Others in the lobby began to laugh and Benjamin heard what it was for everyone in a glass room to make a loud noise at once. It was both deafening and painful.

        “Yes,” she said. “Please! You must deliver those boxes at once. After all, they are not made of glass. It’s a dreadful thing to have boxes made of cardboard. We have some of the finest boxes ever created and they are made of glass. When you deliver the boxes, please make sure the containers are disposed of. We cannot have those here. They must be destroyed. And if you children would like to change into white clothing, that could be arranged as--”

        “We’re only delivering the boxes and then we plan to leave,” Benjamin said quickly.

        “Well, then,” the lady answered. “I suppose you had better be on your way. It’s floor two thousand seven hundred and eighty-eight on your right. And again, do not leave those boxes anywhere. They’re quite out of place.”

        Benjamin felt out of place. He did not know how people could live in an environment that had such strict rules around noise and materials. At home, no one cared what materials made a box. A box was a box. But here, everything had to be glass.

        When they stepped into the glass elevator, Benjamin noticed that the two-thousand seven-hundred and eighty-eighth floor was the highest floor. With the thousands of buttons on the wall indicating each floor, Benjamin was glad this one was so easy to locate. They were alone in a tiny glass room being flung to the top of the skyscraper at a speed faster than Benjamin had experienced. And as they soared higher and higher, Benjamin’s fear of heights became more and more real.

        To distract himself, he turned to Selma.

        “What do people do here?” he asked her.

        “What do you mean? They live,” she responded.

        “No, I mean they make dreams in Castle Ehtahl. What do they make here?”

        “Oh,” she said with an understanding look. “Daydreams.”

Chapter 24: In Which There Is A Glass Tower

        They were nearly three hundred floors in the air when Benjamin’s curiosity for searching for the dream studios caught the best of him. He looked down. And upon looking down, he wished he hadn’t done so because his fear of heights became even more real. It was one thing to stand on the roof of his building in New York. It was another thing to be soaring into the air in a glass container, approaching clouds at a blistering fast speed.

        “Have you been on this elevator before?” he asked Selma nervously.

        “No, why?” she asked.

        “I’m just wondering if it ever breaks.”

        “Breaks?”

        “Yes, like does it ever get stuck?”

        “Oh,” Selma responded as she looked around the glass cube. “I doubt it.”

        “OK,” Benjamin said while taking a deep breath. “That makes me feel a lot better.”

        “It probably crashes to the ground first.”

        “Really?”

        “Into thousands of pieces.”

        “Selma, really?”

        “But you don’t have to worry--”

        “Selma, tell me the truth.”

        “There’s no way you could survive a fall from up here,” Selma said with a chuckle.

        “This isn’t making me feel good.”

        “There probably hasn’t been an accident on this elevator all week.”

        “All week? What about last week?”

        “Tons of accidents!”

        “Selma!”

        “OK,” Selma said as she stopped laughing. “Just think of something happy. You are in a castle that makes daydreams by the way.”

        Benjamin closed his eyes and tried to imagine he was not thousands of feet in the air. He thought about what he would be doing if he were at home at that very moment. Walking home from school, he thought. It was a sunny day and the roads were covered with cars and taxis in New York. A bus a few blocks away picked up some pedestrians while a fire truck passed in the opposite direction. A few birds flew overhead, moving from tree to tree in search of some shade to cool from the heat. A bakery on the other side of the street had an unusually long line for an afternoon while its next door Nordic restaurant was unusually empty. As Benjamin walked further and further, he noticed the people on the street were not talking to one another. No one was talking in the bakery or the restaurant or on the bus for that matter. They were all wearing sunglasses.

        “Wake up!” Selma yelled as she grabbed Benjamin’s shoulder.

        Benjamin opened his eyes and thought he heard thunder and saw lightning in the distance. Selma looked like she had been trying to wake him for a while. She let out a loud sigh and then released his shoulder abruptly before moving to the other side of the elevator.

        “I was daydreaming,” he said.

        “You’ve got to figure out how to stop the nightmares,” she said without looking at him.

        “I don’t have any control of them.”

        “You do, that’s what I’ve been told. You’ve got to figure out how to make the nightmares stop. Or else, bad things will happen.”

        “What kind of bad things?”

        “Things worse than falling thousands of feet down an elevator shaft.”

        “I don’t know. That’s pretty terrible.”

        “Yes. This will be worse. When there are more nightmares, there are fewer dreams.”

        The elevator stopped and Benjamin felt his stomach touch his shoulders. A bell chimed loudly and the doors slowly parted to reveal a small hallway on the two-thousand seven-hundred and eighty-eighth floor. Benjamin and Selma stepped from the elevator into a room shaped like a glass ball that felt like a snow globe. He could see nothing but blue skies, clouds, and the sun. There were no birds flying around at this height. Benjamin and Selma were the only living things around.

        Except for a man wearing a bright white suit standing at attention in front of the only door in the room. He held a gold cane and wore a white top hat slightly tilted to the side. Benjamin thought the man looked more like a cartoon character than a real person.

        “May I help you?” he asked.

        “Hello,” Selma said as she stepped forward. “My friend and I would like to speak with the travel agent. We’d like to go on a trip.”

        “Oh, I’m terribly sorry but the travel agent isn’t here right now.”

        “Oh,” Selma said with disappointment. “When will she return?”

        “When she feels like it I suppose.”

        “Do you know what time that would be?”

        “About ten minutes.”

        “Can we wait until she arrives?”

        “I’m generally not allowed to let people wait. You could come back tomorrow.”

        “We can’t come back tomorrow because we have to leave today.”

        “That is quite a predicament. I suppose you could stay on one condition.”

        “What’s the condition?” Benjamin asked.

        The man twisted his cane around his hand and pointed to a lightbulb at the top of the glass spherical roof. Benjamin and Selma looked in the direction to which he was pointing. And upon seeing what the man was pointing to, Benjamin felt his stomach turn in disapproval.

        “There’s a piece of dust on the outside of the glass. I have a harness and everything but I’ve just not had the time to clean it. It’s so busy here,” said the man.

        Benjamin looked around and imagined that no one had been to this floor in quite some time.

        “If you can remove that bit of dust, I suppose you can stay.”

        “Alright, he’ll do it,” Selma said before asking Benjamin whether he would be willing to do the task.

        “I really think we need to talk about this,” Benjamin said while stepping backwards in disapproval. “Do you have a hose? Maybe we could hose the outside of the glass. You don’t need someone to go outside. Or maybe the wind will blow it away? Have you tried tapping on the glass for a while to knock it loose?

        “Trust me young child, I have tried nothing. But, I am sure the only guaranteed way of removing the piece of dust for good is to go outside and get it. Otherwise, it could just go somewhere else.”

        Selma looked at Benjamin with an expression that felt like she was already preparing the harness. Benjamin looked at Selma with an expression that was purely disapproval with her decision. But if he wanted to go home, he decided he would do it. He didn’t come this way for nothing. At this point in his adventure, he learned to just do things.

        He let out a long sigh and Selma ran to get the harness. She strapped Benjamin in place and Benjamin shook his head slowly with disbelief. He did not want to go outside. Not here.

        “Is there a helmet?” he asked quietly.

        “Of course there’s a helmet!” Selma yelled with enthusiasm as she thumped the helmet on his head. “Look at you! You’re an explorer ready to clean some windows!”
